<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Course">Course</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Course">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bhagirathi_River_at_Gangotri.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Bhagirathi_River_at_Gangotri.JPG/220px-Bhagirathi_River_at_Gangotri.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Bhagirathi_River_at_Gangotri.JPG/330px-Bhagirathi_River_at_Gangotri.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Bhagirathi_River_at_Gangotri.JPG/440px-Bhagirathi_River_at_Gangotri.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bhagirathi_River_at_Gangotri.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bhagirathi River at <a href="/wiki/Gangotri" title="Gangotri">Gangotri</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Devprayag_-_Confluence_of_Bhagirathi_and_Alaknanda.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Devprayag_-_Confluence_of_Bhagirathi_and_Alaknanda.JPG/220px-Devprayag_-_Confluence_of_Bhagirathi_and_Alaknanda.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Devprayag_-_Confluence_of_Bhagirathi_and_Alaknanda.JPG/330px-Devprayag_-_Confluence_of_Bhagirathi_and_Alaknanda.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Devprayag_-_Confluence_of_Bhagirathi_and_Alaknanda.JPG/440px-Devprayag_-_Confluence_of_Bhagirathi_and_Alaknanda.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Devprayag_-_Confluence_of_Bhagirathi_and_Alaknanda.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Devprayag, confluence of Alaknanda (right) and Bhagirathi (left), and beginning of the Ganges proper.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HeadwatersGanges1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2464" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/HeadwatersGanges1.jpg/220px-HeadwatersGanges1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/HeadwatersGanges1.jpg/330px-HeadwatersGanges1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/HeadwatersGanges1.jpg/440px-HeadwatersGanges1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HeadwatersGanges1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Himalayan headwaters of the Ganges River in the <a href="/wiki/Garhwal_division" title="Garhwal division">Garhwal</a> region of Uttarakhand, India.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_Setu_Bridge_in_Patna,_India.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Gandhi_Setu_Bridge_in_Patna%2C_India.jpg/220px-Gandhi_Setu_Bridge_in_Patna%2C_India.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Gandhi_Setu_Bridge_in_Patna%2C_India.jpg/330px-Gandhi_Setu_Bridge_in_Patna%2C_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Gandhi_Setu_Bridge_in_Patna%2C_India.jpg/440px-Gandhi_Setu_Bridge_in_Patna%2C_India.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gandhi_Setu_Bridge_in_Patna,_India.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Gandhi Setu Bridge across the Ganges in Patna, Bihar</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boat_Sailing_up_Padma_River_Bangladesh.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1639" data-file-width="2177" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Boat_Sailing_up_Padma_River_Bangladesh.jpg/220px-Boat_Sailing_up_Padma_River_Bangladesh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Boat_Sailing_up_Padma_River_Bangladesh.jpg/330px-Boat_Sailing_up_Padma_River_Bangladesh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Boat_Sailing_up_Padma_River_Bangladesh.jpg/440px-Boat_Sailing_up_Padma_River_Bangladesh.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boat_Sailing_up_Padma_River_Bangladesh.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sailboat on the main distributary of the Ganges in Bangladesh, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Padma_river" title="Padma river">Padma river</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ganges_Delta_ESA22274217.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3957" data-file-width="3423" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ganges_Delta_ESA22274217.jpeg/220px-Ganges_Delta_ESA22274217.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ganges_Delta_ESA22274217.jpeg/330px-Ganges_Delta_ESA22274217.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ganges_Delta_ESA22274217.jpeg/440px-Ganges_Delta_ESA22274217.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ganges_Delta_ESA22274217.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganges_delta" title="Ganges delta">Ganges delta</a> in a 2020 satellite image.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ganga_at_Sultanganj.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Ganga_at_Sultanganj.jpg/220px-Ganga_at_Sultanganj.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Ganga_at_Sultanganj.jpg/330px-Ganga_at_Sultanganj.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Ganga_at_Sultanganj.jpg/440px-Ganga_at_Sultanganj.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ganga_at_Sultanganj.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Ganges at <a href="/wiki/Sultanganj" title="Sultanganj">Sultanganj</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The upper phase of the river Ganges begins at the <a href="/wiki/Confluence" title="Confluence">confluence</a> of the Bhagirathi and Alaknanda rivers in the town of Devprayag in the Garhwal division of the Indian state of Uttarakhand. The Bhagirathi is considered to be the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Source_(river_or_stream)" title="Source (river or stream)">source</a> in Hindu culture and mythology, although the Alaknanda is longer, and therefore, hydrologically the source stream.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-24">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Penn2001_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Penn2001-25">[20]</a></sup> The headwaters of the Alakananda are formed by snow melt from peaks such as <a href="/wiki/Nanda_Devi" title="Nanda Devi">Nanda Devi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trisul" title="Trisul">Trisul</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kamet" title="Kamet">Kamet</a>. The Bhagirathi rises at the foot of <a href="/wiki/Gangotri_Glacier" title="Gangotri Glacier">Gangotri Glacier</a>, at <a href="/wiki/Gomukh" title="Gomukh">Gomukh</a>, at an elevation of 4,356 m (14,291 ft) and being mythologically referred to as residing in the matted locks of <a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva</a>; symbolically Tapovan, which is a meadow of ethereal beauty at the feet of Mount Shivling, just 5 km (3.1 mi) away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrishna_Murti199119_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrishna_Murti199119-27">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although many small streams comprise the headwaters of the Ganges, the six longest and their five confluences are considered sacred. The six headstreams are the Alaknanda, <a href="/wiki/Dhauliganga_River" title="Dhauliganga River">Dhauliganga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nandakini" title="Nandakini">Nandakini</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pindar_River" title="Pindar River">Pindar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mandakini_River" title="Mandakini River">Mandakini</a> and Bhagirathi. Their confluences, known as the <a href="/wiki/Panch_Prayag" title="Panch Prayag">Panch Prayag</a>, are all along the Alaknanda. They are, in downstream order, <a href="/wiki/Vishnuprayag" title="Vishnuprayag">Vishnuprayag</a>, where the Dhauliganga joins the Alaknanda; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nandprayag" title="Nandprayag">Nandprayag</a>, where the Nandakini joins; <a href="/wiki/Karnaprayag" title="Karnaprayag">Karnaprayag</a>, where the Pindar joins; <a href="/wiki/Rudraprayag" title="Rudraprayag">Rudraprayag</a>, where the Mandakini joins; and finally, Devprayag, where the Bhagirathi joins the Alaknanda to form the Ganges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-24">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>After flowing for 256.90 km (159.63 mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrishna_Murti199119_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrishna_Murti199119-27">[22]</a></sup> through its narrow Himalayan valley, the Ganges emerges from the mountains at <a href="/wiki/Rishikesh" title="Rishikesh">Rishikesh</a>, then <a href="/wiki/Debouch" title="Debouch">debouches</a> onto the Gangetic Plain at the pilgrimage town of <a href="/wiki/Haridwar" title="Haridwar">Haridwar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-24">[19]</a></sup> At Haridwar, a dam diverts some of its waters into the <a href="/wiki/Ganges_Canal" title="Ganges Canal">Ganges Canal</a>, which irrigates the <i><a href="/wiki/Doab" title="Doab">Doab</a></i> region of <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a>, whereas the river, whose course has been roughly southwest until this point, now begins to flow southeast through the plains of northern India.
</p><p>The Ganges river follows a 900 km (560 mi) arching course passing through the cities of Kannauj, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farukhabad" title="Farukhabad">Farukhabad</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kanpur" title="Kanpur">Kanpur</a>. Along the way it is joined by the <a href="/wiki/Ramganga" title="Ramganga">Ramganga</a>, which contributes an average annual flow of about 495 m<sup>3</sup>/s (17,500 cu ft/s) to the river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJainAgarwalSingh2007341_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJainAgarwalSingh2007341-28">[23]</a></sup> The Ganges joins the 1,444 km (897 mi) long River <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yamuna_River" title="Yamuna River">Yamuna</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Triveni_Sangam" title="Triveni Sangam">Triveni Sangam</a> at <a href="/wiki/Allahabad" title="Allahabad">Allahabad</a>, (now Prayagraj) a confluence considered holy in Hinduism. At their confluence the Yamuna is larger than the Ganges contributing about 58.5% of the combined flow,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGupta2007347_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGupta2007347-29">[24]</a></sup> with an average flow of 2,948 m<sup>3</sup>/s (104,100 cu ft/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJainAgarwalSingh2007341_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJainAgarwalSingh2007341-28">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Now flowing east, the river meets the 400 km (250 mi) long <a href="/wiki/Tamsa_River" title="Tamsa River">Tamsa River</a> (also called <i>Tons</i>), which flows north from the <a href="/wiki/Kaimur_Range" title="Kaimur Range">Kaimur Range</a> and contributes an average flow of about 187 m<sup>3</sup>/s (6,600 cu ft/s). After the Tamsa, the 625 km (388 mi) long <a href="/wiki/Gomti_River" title="Gomti River">Gomti River</a> joins, flowing south from the Himalayas. The Gomti contributes an average annual flow of about 234 m<sup>3</sup>/s (8,300 cu ft/s). Then the 1,156 km (718 mi) long <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ghaghara_River" title="Ghaghara River">Ghaghara River</a> (Karnali River), also flowing south from the Himalayas of Tibet through Nepal joins. The Ghaghara (Karnali), with its average annual flow of about 2,991 m<sup>3</sup>/s (105,600 cu ft/s), is the largest tributary of the Ganges by discharge. After the Ghaghara confluence, the Ganges is joined from the south by the 784 km (487 mi) long <a href="/wiki/Son_River" title="Son River">Son River</a>, which contributes about 1,008 m<sup>3</sup>/s (35,600 cu ft/s). The 814 km (506 mi) long <a href="/wiki/Gandaki_River" title="Gandaki River">Gandaki River</a>, then the 729 km (453 mi) long <a href="/wiki/Kosi_River" title="Kosi River">Kosi River</a>, join from the north flowing from Nepal, contributing about 1,654 m<sup>3</sup>/s (58,400 cu ft/s) and 2,166 m<sup>3</sup>/s (76,500 cu ft/s), respectively.  The Kosi is the third largest tributary of the Ganges by discharge, after Ghaghara (Karnali) and Yamuna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJainAgarwalSingh2007341_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJainAgarwalSingh2007341-28">[23]</a></sup> The Kosi merges into the Ganges near Kursela in <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>.
</p><p>Along the way between Allahabad and <a href="/wiki/Malda,_West_Bengal" title="Malda, West Bengal">Malda</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a>, the Ganges river passes the towns of <a href="/wiki/Chunar" title="Chunar">Chunar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mirzapur" title="Mirzapur">Mirzapur</a>, Varanasi, <a href="/wiki/Ghazipur" title="Ghazipur">Ghazipur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arrah" title="Arrah">Ara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patna" title="Patna">Patna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chhapra" title="Chhapra">Chapra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hajipur" title="Hajipur">Hajipur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mokama" title="Mokama">Mokama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Begusarai" title="Begusarai">Begusarai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Munger" title="Munger">Munger</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahibganj" title="Sahibganj">Sahibganj</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rajmahal" title="Rajmahal">Rajmahal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagalpur" title="Bhagalpur">Bhagalpur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ballia" title="Ballia">Ballia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buxar" title="Buxar">Buxar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simaria" title="Simaria">Simaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sultanganj" title="Sultanganj">Sultanganj</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Farakka" title="Farakka">Farakka</a>. At Bhagalpur, the river begins to flow south-southeast and at Farakka, it begins its attrition with the branching away of its first <a href="/wiki/Distributary" title="Distributary">distributary</a>, the 408 km (254 mi) long <a href="/wiki/Hooghly_River" title="Hooghly River">Bhāgirathi-Hooghly</a>, which goes on to become the <a href="/wiki/Hooghly_River" title="Hooghly River">Hooghly River</a>. Just before the border with Bangladesh the <a href="/wiki/Farakka_Barrage" title="Farakka Barrage">Farakka Barrage</a> controls the flow of Ganges, diverting some of the water into a feeder canal linked to the Hooghly for the purpose of keeping it relatively silt-free. The Hooghly River is formed by the confluence of the Bhagirathi River and <a href="/wiki/Ajay_River" title="Ajay River">Ajay River</a> at <a href="/wiki/Katwa" title="Katwa">Katwa</a>, and Hooghly has a number of tributaries of its own. The largest is the <a href="/wiki/Damodar_River" title="Damodar River">Damodar River</a>, which is 625 km (388 mi) long, with a drainage basin of 25,820 km<sup>2</sup> (9,970 sq mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDhungelPun2009215_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDhungelPun2009215-30">[25]</a></sup> The Hooghly River empties into the Bay of Bengal near <a href="/wiki/Sagar_Island" title="Sagar Island">Sagar Island</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChakrabarti2001126–27_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChakrabarti2001126–27-31">[26]</a></sup> Between Malda and the Bay of Bengal, the Hooghly river passes the towns and cities of <a href="/wiki/Murshidabad" title="Murshidabad">Murshidabad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nabadwip" title="Nabadwip">Nabadwip</a>, Kolkata and <a href="/wiki/Howrah" title="Howrah">Howrah</a>.
</p><p>After entering Bangladesh, the main branch of the Ganges river is known as the <a href="/wiki/Padma_River" title="Padma River">Padma</a>. The Padma is joined by the <a href="/wiki/Jamuna_River_(Bangladesh)" title="Jamuna River (Bangladesh)">Jamuna River</a>, the largest distributary of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmaputra" title="Brahmaputra">Brahmaputra</a>. Further downstream, the Padma joins the <a href="/wiki/Meghna_River" title="Meghna River">Meghna River</a>, the converged flow of <a href="/wiki/Surma-Meghna_River_System" title="Surma-Meghna River System">Surma-Meghna River System</a> taking on the Meghna's name as it enters the Meghna Estuary, which empties into the Bay of Bengal. Here it forms the 1,430 by 3,000 km (890 by 1,860 mi) <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Fan" title="Bengal Fan">Bengal Fan</a>, the world's largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Submarine_fan" title="Submarine fan">submarine fan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[27]</a></sup> which alone accounts for 10–20% of the global burial of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_carbon" title="Organic carbon">organic carbon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ganges_Delta" title="Ganges Delta">Ganges Delta</a>, formed mainly by the large, sediment-laden flows of the Ganges and Brahmaputra rivers, is the world's largest delta, at about 64,000 km<sup>2</sup> (25,000 sq mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParua2009_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParua2009-34">[29]</a></sup> It stretches 400 km (250 mi) along the Bay of Bengal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnold2000_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnold2000-35">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Only the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_River" title="Amazon River">Amazon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Congo_River" title="Congo River">Congo</a> rivers have a greater average discharge than the combined flow of the Ganges, the Brahmaputra, and the Surma-Meghna river system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnold2000_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnold2000-35">[30]</a></sup> In full flood only the Amazon is larger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElhance1999156–58_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElhance1999156–58-36">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> lies atop the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Plate" title="Indian Plate">Indian tectonic plate</a>, a minor plate within the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Australian_Plate" title="Indo-Australian Plate">Indo-Australian Plate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAliAitchison2005_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAliAitchison2005-37">[32]</a></sup> Its defining geological processes commenced seventy-five million years ago, when, as a part of the southern supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a>, it began a northeastwards <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">drift</a>—lasting fifty million years—across the then unformed Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAliAitchison2005_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAliAitchison2005-37">[32]</a></sup> The subcontinent's subsequent collision with the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian Plate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">subduction</a> under it, gave rise to the Himalayas, the planet's highest mountain ranges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAliAitchison2005_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAliAitchison2005-37">[32]</a></sup> In the former seabed immediately south of the emerging Himalayas, plate movement created a vast <a href="/wiki/Trough_(geology)" title="Trough (geology)">trough</a>, which, having gradually been filled with sediment borne by the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus</a> and its tributaries and the Ganges and its tributaries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[33]</a></sup> now forms the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Gangetic_Plain" title="Indo-Gangetic Plain">Indo-Gangetic Plain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Indo-Gangetic Plain is geologically known as a foredeep or <a href="/wiki/Foreland_basin" title="Foreland basin">foreland basin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dmowska2003_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dmowska2003-40">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrology">Hydrology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Hydrology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GangesValley%26Plain.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1512" data-file-width="2165" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/GangesValley%26Plain.jpg/220px-GangesValley%26Plain.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/GangesValley%26Plain.jpg/330px-GangesValley%26Plain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/GangesValley%26Plain.jpg/440px-GangesValley%26Plain.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GangesValley%26Plain.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1908 map showing the course of the Ganges and its tributaries.</div></div></div>
<p>Major left-bank tributaries include the Gomti River, Ghaghara River, Gandaki River and Kosi River; major right-bank tributaries include the Yamuna River, Son River, <a href="/wiki/Punpun" title="Punpun">Punpun</a> and Damodar. The hydrology of the Ganges River is very complicated, especially in the Ganges Delta region. One result is different ways to determine the river's length, its <a href="/wiki/Discharge_(hydrology)" title="Discharge (hydrology)">discharge</a>, and the size of its <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:River_Ganga_with_Howrah_bridge_in_the_backdrop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/River_Ganga_with_Howrah_bridge_in_the_backdrop.jpg/220px-River_Ganga_with_Howrah_bridge_in_the_backdrop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/River_Ganga_with_Howrah_bridge_in_the_backdrop.jpg/330px-River_Ganga_with_Howrah_bridge_in_the_backdrop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/River_Ganga_with_Howrah_bridge_in_the_backdrop.jpg/440px-River_Ganga_with_Howrah_bridge_in_the_backdrop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:River_Ganga_with_Howrah_bridge_in_the_backdrop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The River Ganges at Kolkata, with <a href="/wiki/Howrah_Bridge" title="Howrah Bridge">Howrah Bridge</a> in the background</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lower_Ganges_in_Lakshmipur,_Bangladesh.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Lower_Ganges_in_Lakshmipur%2C_Bangladesh.jpg/220px-Lower_Ganges_in_Lakshmipur%2C_Bangladesh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Lower_Ganges_in_Lakshmipur%2C_Bangladesh.jpg/330px-Lower_Ganges_in_Lakshmipur%2C_Bangladesh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Lower_Ganges_in_Lakshmipur%2C_Bangladesh.jpg/440px-Lower_Ganges_in_Lakshmipur%2C_Bangladesh.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lower_Ganges_in_Lakshmipur,_Bangladesh.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lower Ganges in Lakshmipur, Bangladesh</div></div></div>
<p>The name <i>Ganges</i> is used for the river between the confluence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bhagirathi" title="Bhagirathi">Bhagirathi</a> and Alaknanda rivers, in the Himalayas, and the first <a href="/wiki/River_bifurcation" title="River bifurcation">bifurcation</a> of the river, near the Farakka Barrage and the India-Bangladesh Border. The length of the Ganges is frequently said to be slightly over 2,600 km (1,600 mi) long, about 2,601 km (1,616 mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merriam-WebsterInc1997_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Merriam-WebsterInc1997-41">[36]</a></sup> 2,525 km (1,569 mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGupta2007347_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGupta2007347-29">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJainAgarwalSingh2007334–342_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJainAgarwalSingh2007334–342-42">[37]</a></sup> or 2,650 km (1,650 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBerga20061304_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBerga20061304-43">[38]</a></sup> In these cases the river's source is usually assumed to be the source of the Bhagirathi River, Gangotri Glacier at Gomukh and its mouth being the mouth of the Meghna River on the Bay of Bengal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGupta2007347_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGupta2007347-29">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merriam-WebsterInc1997_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Merriam-WebsterInc1997-41">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJainAgarwalSingh2007334–342_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJainAgarwalSingh2007334–342-42">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBerga20061304_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBerga20061304-43">[38]</a></sup> Sometimes the source of the Ganges is considered to be at Haridwar, where its Himalayan headwater streams debouch onto the Gangetic Plain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDhungelPun2009210_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDhungelPun2009210-44">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>In some cases, the length of the Ganges is given by its Hooghly River distributary, which is longer than its main outlet via the Meghna River, resulting in a total length of about 2,704 km (1,680 mi), if taken from the source of the Bhagirathi,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParua2009_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParua2009-34">[29]</a></sup> or 2,321.50 km (1,442.51 mi), if from Haridwar to the Hooghly's mouth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDhungelPun2009_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDhungelPun2009-45">[40]</a></sup> In other cases the length is said to be about 2,304 km (1,432 mi), from the source of the Bhagirathi to the Bangladesh border, where its name changes to <i>Padma</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMirza2004_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMirza2004-46">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>For similar reasons, sources differ over the size of the river's drainage basin. The basin covers parts of four countries, India, <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">China</a>, and Bangladesh; eleven Indian states, <a href="/wiki/Himachal_Pradesh" title="Himachal Pradesh">Himachal Pradesh</a>, Uttarakhand, Uttar Pradesh, Madhya Pradesh, <a href="/wiki/Chhattisgarh" title="Chhattisgarh">Chhattisgarh</a>, Bihar, <a href="/wiki/Jharkhand" title="Jharkhand">Jharkhand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_India" title="Punjab, India">Punjab</a>, Haryana, <a href="/wiki/Rajasthan" title="Rajasthan">Rajasthan</a>, West Bengal, and the Union Territory of <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[42]</a></sup> The Ganges basin, including the delta but not the Brahmaputra or Meghna basins, is about 1,080,000 km<sup>2</sup> (420,000 sq mi), of which 861,000 km<sup>2</sup> (332,000 sq mi) is in India (about 80%), 140,000 km<sup>2</sup> (54,000 sq mi) in Nepal (13%), 46,000 km<sup>2</sup> (18,000 sq mi) in Bangladesh (4%), and 33,000 km<sup>2</sup> (13,000 sq mi) in China (3%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuvedī200561_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuvedī200561-48">[43]</a></sup> Sometimes the Ganges and Brahmaputra–Meghna drainage basins are combined for a total of about 1,600,000 km<sup>2</sup> (620,000 sq mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElhance1999156–58_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElhance1999156–58-36">[31]</a></sup> or 1,621,000 km<sup>2</sup> (626,000 sq mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnold2000_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnold2000-35">[30]</a></sup> The combined Ganges-Brahmaputra-Meghna basin (abbreviated GBM or GMB) drainage basin is spread across Bangladesh, <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>, India, Nepal, and China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ServatSystems2002_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-ServatSystems2002-49">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Ganges basin ranges from the Himalaya and the <a href="/wiki/Transhimalaya" title="Transhimalaya">Transhimalaya</a> in the north, to the northern slopes of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vindhya" title="Vindhya">Vindhya</a> range in the south, from the eastern slopes of the <a href="/wiki/Aravalli_Range" title="Aravalli Range">Aravalli</a> in the west to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chota_Nagpur_plateau" title="Chota Nagpur plateau">Chota Nagpur plateau</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunderbans" title="Sunderbans">Sunderbans</a> delta in the east. A significant portion of the discharge from the Ganges comes from the Himalayan mountain system. Within the Himalaya, the Ganges basin spreads almost 1,200 km from the Yamuna-Satluj divide along the Simla ridge forming the boundary with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus" title="Indus">Indus</a> basin in the west to the Singalila Ridge along the Nepal-Sikkim border forming the boundary with the Brahmaputra basin in the east. This section of the Himalaya contains 9 of the 14 highest peaks in the world over 8,000m in height, including <a href="/wiki/Mount_Everest" title="Mount Everest">Mount Everest</a> which is the high point of the Ganges basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evepkbgr_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-evepkbgr-50">[45]</a></sup> The other peaks over 8,000m in the basin are <a href="/wiki/Kangchenjunga" title="Kangchenjunga">Kangchenjunga</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kangchpkbgr_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-kangchpkbgr-51">[46]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lhotse" title="Lhotse">Lhotse</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lhotpkbgr_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-lhotpkbgr-52">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Makalu" title="Makalu">Makalu</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-makalpkbgr_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-makalpkbgr-53">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cho_Oyu" title="Cho Oyu">Cho Oyu</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-choopkbgr_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-choopkbgr-54">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dhaulagiri" title="Dhaulagiri">Dhaulagiri</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dhaulapkbgr_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-dhaulapkbgr-55">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Manaslu" title="Manaslu">Manaslu</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manaslpkbgr_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-manaslpkbgr-56">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Annapurna" title="Annapurna">Annapurna</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-annappkbgr_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-annappkbgr-57">[52]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Shishapangma" title="Shishapangma">Shishapangma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shishpkbgr_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-shishpkbgr-58">[53]</a></sup> The Himalayan portion of the basin includes the south-eastern portion of the state of Himachal Pradesh, the entire state of Uttarakhand, the entire country of Nepal and the extreme north-western portion of the state of West Bengal.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The discharge of the Ganges also differs by source. Frequently, discharge is described for the mouth of the Meghna River, thus combining the Ganges with the Brahmaputra and Meghna. This results in a total average annual discharge of about 38,000 m<sup>3</sup>/s (1,300,000 cu ft/s),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnold2000_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnold2000-35">[30]</a></sup> or 42,470 m<sup>3</sup>/s (1,500,000 cu ft/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParua2009_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParua2009-34">[29]</a></sup> In other cases the average annual discharges of the Ganges, Brahmaputra, and Meghna are given separately, at about 16,650 m<sup>3</sup>/s (588,000 cu ft/s) for the Ganges, about 19,820 m<sup>3</sup>/s (700,000 cu ft/s) for the Brahmaputra, and about 5,100 m<sup>3</sup>/s (180,000 cu ft/s) for the Meghna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJainAgarwalSingh2007334–342_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJainAgarwalSingh2007334–342-42">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hardinge_Bridge_Bangladesh_(4).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Hardinge_Bridge_Bangladesh_%284%29.JPG/220px-Hardinge_Bridge_Bangladesh_%284%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Hardinge_Bridge_Bangladesh_%284%29.JPG/330px-Hardinge_Bridge_Bangladesh_%284%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Hardinge_Bridge_Bangladesh_%284%29.JPG/440px-Hardinge_Bridge_Bangladesh_%284%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hardinge_Bridge_Bangladesh_(4).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hardinge_Bridge" title="Hardinge Bridge">Hardinge Bridge</a>, Bangladesh, crosses the Ganges-Padma River. It is one of the key sites for measuring streamflow and discharge on the lower Ganges.</div></div></div>
<p>The maximum peak discharge of the Ganges, as recorded at <a href="/wiki/Hardinge_Bridge" title="Hardinge Bridge">Hardinge Bridge</a> in Bangladesh, exceeded 70,000 m<sup>3</sup>/s (2,500,000 cu ft/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrishna_Murti199110_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrishna_Murti199110-59">[54]</a></sup> The minimum recorded at the same place was about 180 m<sup>3</sup>/s (6,400 cu ft/s), in 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESalmanUprety2002133_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESalmanUprety2002133-60">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>The hydrologic cycle in the Ganges basin is governed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monsoon_of_Indian_subcontinent" title="Monsoon of Indian subcontinent">Southwest Monsoon</a>. About 84% of the total rainfall occurs in the monsoon from June to September. Consequently, <a href="/wiki/Streamflow" title="Streamflow">streamflow</a> in the Ganges is highly seasonal. The average dry season to monsoon discharge ratio is about 1:6, as measured at <a href="/wiki/Hardinge_Bridge" title="Hardinge Bridge">Hardinge Bridge</a>. This strong seasonal variation underlies many problems of land and water resource development in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMirza2004_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMirza2004-46">[41]</a></sup> The seasonality of flow is so acute it can cause both drought and <a href="/wiki/Flood" title="Flood">floods</a>. Bangladesh, in particular, frequently experiences drought during the dry season and regularly suffers extreme floods during the monsoon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESalmanUprety2002133_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESalmanUprety2002133-60">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Ganges Delta, many large rivers come together, both merging and <a href="/wiki/River_bifurcation" title="River bifurcation">bifurcating</a> in a complicated network of <a href="/wiki/Channel_(geography)" title="Channel (geography)">channels</a>. The two largest rivers, the Ganges and Brahmaputra, both split into distributary channels, the largest of which merge with other large rivers before themselves joining the Bay of Bengal. But this current channel pattern was not always the case. Over time the rivers in Ganges Delta have often <a href="/wiki/Avulsion_(river)" title="Avulsion (river)">changed course</a>, sometimes altering the network of channels in significant ways.
</p><p>Before the late 12th century the Bhagirathi-Hooghly distributary was the main channel of the Ganges and the Padma was only a minor spill-channel. The main flow of the river reached the sea not via the modern Hooghly River but rather by the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Adi_Ganga&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Adi Ganga (page does not exist)">Adi Ganga</a>. Between the 12th and 16th centuries, the Bhagirathi-Hooghly and Padma channels were more or less equally significant. After the 16th century, the Padma grew to become the main channel of the Ganges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChakrabarti2001126–27_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChakrabarti2001126–27-31">[26]</a></sup> It is thought that the Bhagirathi-Hooghly became increasingly choked with silt, causing the main flow of the Ganges to shift to the southeast and the Padma River. By the end of the 18th century, the Padma had become the main distributary of the Ganges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParua2009_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParua2009-34">[29]</a></sup> One result of this shift to the Padma was that the Ganges now joined the Meghna and Brahmaputra rivers before emptying into the Bay of Bengal. The present confluence of the Ganges and Meghna was formed very recently, about 150 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Catling1992_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Catling1992-61">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Also near the end of the 18th century, the course of the lower Brahmaputra changed dramatically, significantly altering its relationship with the Ganges. In 1787 there was a great flood on the <a href="/wiki/Teesta_River" title="Teesta River">Teesta River</a>, which at the time was a tributary of the Ganges-Padma River. The flood of 1787 caused the Teesta to undergo a sudden change course, an <a href="/wiki/Avulsion_(river)" title="Avulsion (river)">avulsion</a>, shifting east to join the Brahmaputra and causing the Brahmaputra to shift its course south, cutting a new channel. This new main channel of the Brahmaputra is called the Jamuna River. It flows south to join the Ganges-Padma. During ancient times, the main flow of the Brahmaputra was more easterly, passing by the city of <a href="/wiki/Mymensingh" title="Mymensingh">Mymensingh</a> and joining the Meghna River. Today this channel is a small distributary but retains the name Brahmaputra, sometimes Old Brahmaputra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica-Brahmaputra_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-Brahmaputra-62">[57]</a></sup> The site of the old Brahmaputra-Meghna confluence, in the locality of <a href="/wiki/Langalbandh" title="Langalbandh">Langalbandh</a>, is still considered sacred by Hindus. Near the confluence is a major early historic site called <a href="/wiki/Wari-Bateshwar_ruins" title="Wari-Bateshwar ruins">Wari-Bateshwar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChakrabarti2001126–127_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChakrabarti2001126–127-63">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the rainy season of 1809, the lower channel of the Bhagirathi, leading to Kolkata, had been entirely shut; but in the following year it opened again and was nearly of the same size as the upper channel but both however suffered a considerable diminution, owing probably to the new communication opened below the Jalanggi on the upper channel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first European traveller to mention the Ganges was the Greek envoy <a href="/wiki/Megasthenes" title="Megasthenes">Megasthenes</a> (ca. 350–290 BCE). He did so several times in his work <a href="/wiki/Indica_(Megasthenes)" title="Indica (Megasthenes)">Indica</a>: "India, again, possesses many rivers both large and navigable, which, having their sources in the mountains which stretch along the northern frontier, traverse the level country, and not a few of these, after uniting with each other, fall into the river called the Ganges. Now this river, which at its source is 30 <a href="/wiki/Stadion_(unit)" title="Stadion (unit)">stadia</a> broad, flows from north to south, and empties its waters into the ocean forming the eastern boundary of the <a href="/wiki/Gangaridai" title="Gangaridai">Gangaridai</a>, a nation which possesses a vast force of the largest-sized elephants." (Diodorus II.37).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1951 a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharing_the_water_of_the_Ganges" title="Sharing the water of the Ganges">water sharing dispute</a> arose between India and East Pakistan (now Bangladesh) after India declared its intention to build the Farakka Barrage. The original purpose of the barrage, which was completed in 1975, was to divert up to 1,100 m<sup>3</sup>/s (39,000 cu ft/s) of water from the Ganges to the Bhagirathi-Hooghly distributary to restore navigability at the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Kolkata" title="Port of Kolkata">Port of Kolkata</a>. It was assumed that during the worst dry season the Ganges flow would be around 1,400 to 1,600 m<sup>3</sup>/s (49,000 to 57,000 cu ft/s), thus leaving 280 to 420 m<sup>3</sup>/s (9,900 to 14,800 cu ft/s) for the then East Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESalmanUprety2002136–137_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESalmanUprety2002136–137-66">[61]</a></sup> East Pakistan objected and a protracted dispute ensued. In 1996 a 30-year treaty was signed with Bangladesh. The terms of the agreement are complicated, but in essence, they state that if the Ganges flow at Farakka was less than 2,000 m<sup>3</sup>/s (71,000 cu ft/s) then India and Bangladesh would each receive 50% of the water, with each receiving at least 1,000 m<sup>3</sup>/s (35,000 cu ft/s) for alternating ten-day periods. However, within a year the flow at Farakka fell to levels far below the historic average, making it impossible to implement the guaranteed sharing of water. In March 1997, flow of the Ganges in Bangladesh dropped to its lowest ever, 180 m<sup>3</sup>/s (6,400 cu ft/s). Dry season flows returned to normal levels in the years following, but efforts were made to address the problem. One plan is for another barrage to be built in Bangladesh at <a href="/wiki/Pangsha_Upazila" title="Pangsha Upazila">Pangsha</a>, west of <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dhaka</a>. This barrage would help Bangladesh better utilize its share of the waters of the Ganges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[f]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_and_cultural_significance">Religious and cultural significance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Religious and cultural significance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganga_in_Hinduism" title="Ganga in Hinduism">Ganga in Hinduism</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Embodiment_of_sacredness">Embodiment of sacredness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Embodiment of sacredness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IndianWomanFloatingLampsGanges.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="507" data-file-width="712" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/IndianWomanFloatingLampsGanges.jpg/220px-IndianWomanFloatingLampsGanges.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/IndianWomanFloatingLampsGanges.jpg/330px-IndianWomanFloatingLampsGanges.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/IndianWomanFloatingLampsGanges.jpg/440px-IndianWomanFloatingLampsGanges.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IndianWomanFloatingLampsGanges.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chromolithograph, <i>Indian woman floating lamps on the Ganges</i>, by William Simpson, 1867</div></div></div>
<p>The Ganges is a sacred river to Hindus along every fragment of its length. All along its course, Hindus bathe in its waters,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p212_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p212-68">[62]</a></sup> paying homage to their ancestors and their gods by cupping the water in their hands, lifting it, and letting it fall back into the river; they offer flowers and rose petals and float shallow clay dishes filled with oil and lit with wicks (diyas).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p212_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p212-68">[62]</a></sup> On the journey back home from the Ganges, they carry small quantities of river water with them for use in rituals; Ganga Jal, literally "the water of the Ganges".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p212-213_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p212-213-69">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Ganges is the embodiment of all <a href="/wiki/Sacred_waters" title="Sacred waters">sacred waters</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hindu_mythology" title="Hindu mythology">Hindu mythology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p214_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p214-70">[64]</a></sup> Local rivers are said to be <i>like</i> the Ganges and are sometimes called the local Ganges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p214_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p214-70">[64]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Godavari_River" title="Godavari River">Godavari River</a> of <a href="/wiki/Maharashtra" title="Maharashtra">Maharashtra</a> in Western India is called the Ganges of the South or the 'Dakshin Ganga'; the Godavari is the Ganges that was led by the sage <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Maharishi" title="Gautama Maharishi">Gautama</a> to flow through Central India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p214_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p214-70">[64]</a></sup> The Ganges is invoked whenever water is used in Hindu ritual and is therefore present in all sacred waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p214_70-3"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p214-70">[64]</a></sup> Despite this, nothing is more stirring for a Hindu than a dip in the actual river, which is thought to remit sins, especially at one of the famous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tirtha_and_Kshetra" title="Tirtha and Kshetra">tirthas</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gangothri" title="Gangothri">Gangotri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haridwar" title="Haridwar">Haridwar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Triveni_Sangam" title="Triveni Sangam">Triveni Sangam</a> at Allahabad, or Varanasi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p214_70-4"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p214-70">[64]</a></sup> The symbolic and religious importance of the Ganges is one of the few things that Hindus, even their skeptics, have agreed upon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p214-215_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p214-215-71">[65]</a></sup> Jawaharlal Nehru, a religious iconoclast himself, asked for a handful of his ashes to be thrown into the Ganges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p214-215_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p214-215-71">[65]</a></sup> "The Ganga", he wrote in his will, "is the river of India, beloved of her people, round which are intertwined her racial memories, her hopes and fears, her songs of triumph, her victories and her defeats. She has been a symbol of India's age-long culture and civilization, ever-changing, ever-flowing, and yet ever the same Ganga."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p214-215_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p214-215-71">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Avatarana_-_Descent_of_Ganges"><i>Avatarana</i> - Descent of Ganges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Avatarana - Descent of Ganges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ravi_Varma-Descent_of_Ganga.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="582" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Ravi_Varma-Descent_of_Ganga.jpg/170px-Ravi_Varma-Descent_of_Ganga.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Ravi_Varma-Descent_of_Ganga.jpg/255px-Ravi_Varma-Descent_of_Ganga.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Ravi_Varma-Descent_of_Ganga.jpg/340px-Ravi_Varma-Descent_of_Ganga.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ravi_Varma-Descent_of_Ganga.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Descent of Ganga</i>, painting by <a href="/wiki/Raja_Ravi_Varma" title="Raja Ravi Varma">Raja Ravi Varma</a> c. 1910</div></div></div>
<p>In late May or early June every year, Hindus celebrate the <i>karunasiri</i> and the rise of the Ganges from earth to heaven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p144_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p144-72">[66]</a></sup> The day of the celebration, <i>Ganga Dashahara</i>, the <i>Dashami</i> (tenth day) of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waxing_moon" title="Waxing moon">waxing moon</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_calendar" title="Hindu calendar">Hindu calendar</a> month <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jyestha" title="Jyestha">Jyestha</a>, brings throngs of bathers to the banks of the river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p144_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p144-72">[66]</a></sup> A dip in the Ganges on this day is said to rid the bather of ten sins (dasha = Sanskrit "ten"; hara = to destroy) or ten lifetimes of sins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p144_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p144-72">[66]</a></sup> Those who cannot journey to the river, however, can achieve the same results by bathing in any nearby body of water, which, for the true believer, takes on all the attributes of the Ganges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p144_72-3"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p144-72">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>karunasiri</i> is an old theme in Hinduism with a number of different versions of the story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p144_72-4"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p144-72">[66]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedic</a> version, <a href="/wiki/Indra" title="Indra">Indra</a>, the Lord of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swarga" title="Swarga">Swarga</a> (<a href="/wiki/Heaven" title="Heaven">Heaven</a>) slays the celestial serpent, <a href="/wiki/Vritra" title="Vritra">Vritra</a>, releasing the celestial liquid, <i><a href="/wiki/Soma_(drink)" title="Soma (drink)">soma</a></i>, or the nectar of the gods which then plunges to the earth and waters it with sustenance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p144_72-5"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p144-72">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vaishnava" title="Vaishnava">Vaishnava</a> version of the myth, the heavenly waters were then a river called <i>Vishnupadi</i> (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>: "from the foot of Vishnu").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p144_72-6"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p144-72">[66]</a></sup> As Lord Vishnu as the avatar <a href="/wiki/Vamana" title="Vamana">Vamana</a> completes his celebrated three strides —of earth, sky, and heaven— he stubs his toe on the vault of heaven, punches open a hole and releases the <i>Vishnupadi</i>, which until now had been circling the cosmic egg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p144-145_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p144-145-73">[67]</a></sup> Flowing out of the vault, she plummets down to Indra's heaven, where she is received by <a href="/wiki/Dhruva" title="Dhruva">Dhruva</a>, once a steadfast worshipper of Vishnu, now fixed in the sky as the <a href="/wiki/Pole_star" title="Pole star">Pole star</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p144-145_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p144-145-73">[67]</a></sup> Next, she streams across the sky forming the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> and arrives on the moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p144-145_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p144-145-73">[67]</a></sup> She then flows down earthwards to <a href="/wiki/Brahma" title="Brahma">Brahma</a>'s realm, a divine lotus atop <a href="/wiki/Mount_Meru" title="Mount Meru">Mount Meru</a>, whose petals form the earthly continents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p144-145_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p144-145-73">[67]</a></sup> There, the divine waters break up, with one stream, the Bhagirathi, flowing down one petal into <a href="/wiki/Names_for_India" title="Names for India">Bharatvarsha</a> (India) as the Ganges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p144-145_73-4"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p144-145-73">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is <a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva</a>, however, among the major deities of the Hindu pantheon, who appears in the most widely known version of the <i>avatarana</i> story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p145_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p145-74">[68]</a></sup> Told and retold in the <i><a href="/wiki/Ramayana" title="Ramayana">Ramayana</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a></i> and several <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranas</a>, the story begins with a sage, <a href="/wiki/Kapila" title="Kapila">Kapila</a>, whose intense meditation has been disturbed by the sixty thousand sons of King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sagara_(Vedic_king)" title="Sagara (Vedic king)">Sagara</a>. Livid at being disturbed, Kapila sears them with his angry gaze, reduces them to ashes, and dispatches them to the netherworld. Only the waters of the Ganges, then in heaven, can bring the dead sons their salvation. A descendant of these sons, King <a href="/wiki/Bhagiratha" title="Bhagiratha">Bhagiratha</a>, anxious to restore his ancestors, undertakes rigorous penance and is eventually granted the prize of Ganges's descent from heaven. However, since her turbulent force would also shatter the earth, Bhagiratha persuades Shiva in his abode on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kailash" title="Mount Kailash">Mount Kailash</a> to receive the Ganges in the coils of his tangled hair and break her fall. The Ganges descends is tamed in Shiva's locks, and arrives in the Himalayas. She is then led by the waiting Bhagiratha down into the plains at Haridwar, across the plains first to the confluence with the Yamuna at Prayag and then to Varanasi, and eventually to Ganges Sagar (Ganges delta), where she meets the ocean, sinks to the netherworld, and saves the sons of Sagara.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p145_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p145-74">[68]</a></sup> In honour of Bhagirath's pivotal role in the <i>avatarana</i>, the source stream of the Ganges in the Himalayas is named Bhagirathi, (Sanskrit, "of Bhagiratha").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p145_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p145-74">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Redemption_of_the_Dead">Redemption of the Dead</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Redemption of the Dead">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HinduCremationVaranasi1903.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="486" data-file-width="428" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/HinduCremationVaranasi1903.jpg/220px-HinduCremationVaranasi1903.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/HinduCremationVaranasi1903.jpg/330px-HinduCremationVaranasi1903.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/b4/HinduCremationVaranasi1903.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HinduCremationVaranasi1903.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Preparations for cremations on the banks of the Ganges in Varanasi, 1903. The dead are being bathed, wrapped in cloth, and covered with wood.  The photograph has a caption, "Who dies in the waters of the Ganges obtains heaven."</div></div></div><p>
As the Ganges had descended from heaven to earth, she is also considered the vehicle of <i>ascent</i>, from earth to heaven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p145-146_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p145-146-75">[69]</a></sup> As the <i>Triloka-patha-gamini</i>, (Sanskrit: <i>triloka</i>= "three worlds", <i>patha</i> = "road", <i>gamini</i> = "one who travels") of the Hindu tradition, she flows in <a href="/wiki/Svarga" title="Svarga">heaven</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prithvi" title="Prithvi">earth</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Patala" title="Patala">netherworld</a>, and, consequently, is a "tirtha" or crossing point of all beings, the living as well as the dead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p145-146_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p145-146-75">[69]</a></sup> It is for this reason that the story of the <i>avatarana</i> is told at <i><a href="/wiki/%C5%9Ar%C4%81ddha" title="Śrāddha">Shraddha</a></i> ceremonies for the deceased in Hinduism, and Ganges water is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vedic_rituals_after_death" title="Vedic rituals after death">Vedic rituals after death</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p145-146_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p145-146-75">[69]</a></sup> Among all hymns devoted to the Ganges, there are none more popular than the ones expressing the worshipper's wish to breathe his last surrounded by her waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p145-146_75-3"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p145-146-75">[69]</a></sup> The <i>Gangashtakam</i> expresses this longing fervently:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p145-146_75-4"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p145-146-75">[69]</a></sup></p><blockquote><p>O Mother! ... Necklace adorning the worlds!<br/>Banner rising to heaven!<br/>I ask that I may leave of this body on your banks,<br/>Drinking your water, rolling in your waves,<br/>Remembering your name, bestowing my gaze upon you.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[70]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>No place along her banks is more longed for at the moment of death by Hindus than Varanasi, the Great Cremation Ground, or <i><a href="/wiki/Shmashana" title="Shmashana">Mahashmshana</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p145-146_75-5"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p145-146-75">[69]</a></sup> Those who are lucky enough to die in Varanasi, are cremated on the banks of the Ganges, and are granted instant salvation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p215_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p215-77">[71]</a></sup> If the death has occurred elsewhere, salvation can be achieved by immersing the ashes in the Ganges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p215_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p215-77">[71]</a></sup> If the ashes have been immersed in another body of water, a relative can still gain salvation for the deceased by journeying to the Ganges, if possible during the lunar "fortnight of the ancestors" in the Hindu calendar month of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ashwin" title="Ashwin">Ashwin</a> (September or October), and performing the <i>Shraddha</i> rites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p215_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p215-77">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hindus also perform <i><a href="/wiki/Pinda_(riceball)" title="Pinda (riceball)">pinda</a> pradana</i>, a rite for the dead, in which balls of rice and sesame seed are offered to the Ganges while the names of the deceased relatives are recited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p215-216_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p215-216-78">[72]</a></sup> Every sesame seed in every ball thus offered, according to one story, assures a thousand years of heavenly salvation for each relative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p215-216_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p215-216-78">[72]</a></sup> Indeed, the Ganges is so important in the rituals after death that the <i>Mahabharata</i>, in one of its popular <i>ślokas</i>, says, "If only (one) bone of a (deceased) person should touch the water of the Ganges, that person shall dwell honoured in heaven."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[73]</a></sup> As if to illustrate this truism, the <i>Kashi Khanda</i> (Varanasi Chapter) of the <a href="/wiki/Skanda_Purana" title="Skanda Purana">Skanda Purana</a> recounts the remarkable story of <i>Vahika</i>, a profligate and unrepentant sinner, who is killed by a tiger in the forest. His soul arrives before <a href="/wiki/Yama_(Hinduism)" title="Yama (Hinduism)">Yama</a>, the Lord of Death, to be judged for the afterworld. Having no compensating virtue, Vahika's soul is at once dispatched to <a href="/wiki/Naraka_(Hinduism)" title="Naraka (Hinduism)">hell</a>. While this is happening, his body on earth, however, is being picked at by vultures, one of whom flies away with a foot bone. Another bird comes after the vulture, and in fighting him off, the vulture accidentally drops the bone into the Ganges below. Blessed by this event, Vahika, on his way to hell, is rescued by a celestial chariot which takes him instead to heaven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p216_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p216-80">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Purifying_Ganges">The Purifying Ganges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: The Purifying Ganges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BathingGhatBanares1885.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="558" data-file-width="712" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/02/BathingGhatBanares1885.jpg/220px-BathingGhatBanares1885.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/02/BathingGhatBanares1885.jpg/330px-BathingGhatBanares1885.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/02/BathingGhatBanares1885.jpg/440px-BathingGhatBanares1885.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BathingGhatBanares1885.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Women and children at a bathing <a href="/wiki/Ghat" title="Ghat">ghat</a> on the Ganges in Banares (Varanasi), 1885.</div></div></div>
<p>Hindus consider the waters of the Ganges to be both pure and purifying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p216-217_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p216-217-81">[75]</a></sup>  Regardless of all scientific understanding of its waters, the Ganges is always ritually and symbolically pure in Hindu culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p216-217_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p216-217-81">[75]</a></sup>  Nothing reclaims order from disorder more than the waters of the Ganga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p217_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p217-82">[76]</a></sup>  Moving water, as in a river, is considered purifying in Hindu culture because it is thought to both absorb impurities and take them away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p217_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p217-82">[76]</a></sup>  The swiftly moving Ganga, especially in its upper reaches, where a bather has to grasp an anchored chain to not be carried away, is especially purifying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p217_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p217-82">[76]</a></sup>  What the Ganges removes, however, is not necessarily physical dirt, but symbolic dirt; it wipes away the sins of the bather, not just of the present, but of a lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p217_82-3"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p217-82">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>
A popular paean to the Ganga is the <i>Ganga Lahiri</i> composed by a 17th-century poet Jagannatha who, legend has it, was turned out of his Hindu <a href="/wiki/Brahmin" title="Brahmin">Brahmin</a> caste for carrying on an affair with a Muslim woman. Having attempted futilely to be rehabilitated within the Hindu fold, the poet finally appeals to Ganga, the hope of the hopeless, and the comforter of last resort. Along with his beloved, Jagannatha sits at the top of the flight of steps leading to the water at the famous <i>Panchganga</i> <a href="/wiki/Ghat" title="Ghat">Ghat</a> in Varanasi. As he recites each verse of the poem, the water of the Ganges rises one step until in the end it envelops the lovers and carries them away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p217_82-4"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p217-82">[76]</a></sup>  "I come to you as a child to his mother", begins the <i>Ganga Lahiri</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p218_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p218-83">[77]</a></sup> </p><blockquote><p> I come as an orphan to you, moist with love.<br/>I come without refuge to you, giver of sacred rest.<br/>I come a fallen man to you, uplifter of all.<br/>I come undone by disease to you, the perfect physician.<br/>I come, my heart dry with thirst, to you, ocean of sweet wine.<br/>Do with me whatever you will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p218_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p218-83">[77]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span id="Consort.2C_Shakti.2C_and_Mother"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Consort,_Shakti,_and_Mother">Consort, Shakti, and Mother</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Consort, Shakti, and Mother">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ganga is a consort to all three major male deities of Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p219_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p219-84">[78]</a></sup>  As Brahma's partner she always travels with him in the form of water in his <a href="/wiki/Kamandalu" title="Kamandalu">kamandalu</a> (water-pot).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p219_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p219-84">[78]</a></sup>  She is also <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a>'s consort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p219_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p219-84">[78]</a></sup>  Not only does she emanate from his foot as <i>Vishnupadi</i> in the <i>avatarana</i> story, but is also, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarasvati" title="Sarasvati">Sarasvati</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lakshmi" title="Lakshmi">Lakshmi</a>, one of his co-wives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p219_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p219-84">[78]</a></sup>  In one popular story, envious of being outdone by each other, the co-wives begin to quarrel. While Lakshmi attempts to mediate the quarrel, Ganga and Sarasvati, heap misfortune on each other. They curse each other to become rivers, and to carry within them, by washing, the sins of their human worshippers. Soon their husband, Vishnu, arrives and decides to calm the situation by separating the goddesses. He orders Sarasvati to become the wife of Brahma, Ganga to become the wife of Shiva, and Lakshmi, as the blameless conciliator, to remain as his own wife. Ganga and Sarasvati, however, are so distraught at this dispensation, and wail so loudly, that Vishnu is forced to take back his words. Consequently, in their lives as rivers they are still thought to be with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p146_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p146-85">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shiva_Bearing_the_Descent_of_the_Ganges_River,_folio_from_a_Hindi_manuscript_by_the_saint_Narayan_LACMA_M.86.345.6.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2100" data-file-width="1542" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Shiva_Bearing_the_Descent_of_the_Ganges_River%2C_folio_from_a_Hindi_manuscript_by_the_saint_Narayan_LACMA_M.86.345.6.jpg/170px-Shiva_Bearing_the_Descent_of_the_Ganges_River%2C_folio_from_a_Hindi_manuscript_by_the_saint_Narayan_LACMA_M.86.345.6.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Shiva_Bearing_the_Descent_of_the_Ganges_River%2C_folio_from_a_Hindi_manuscript_by_the_saint_Narayan_LACMA_M.86.345.6.jpg/255px-Shiva_Bearing_the_Descent_of_the_Ganges_River%2C_folio_from_a_Hindi_manuscript_by_the_saint_Narayan_LACMA_M.86.345.6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Shiva_Bearing_the_Descent_of_the_Ganges_River%2C_folio_from_a_Hindi_manuscript_by_the_saint_Narayan_LACMA_M.86.345.6.jpg/340px-Shiva_Bearing_the_Descent_of_the_Ganges_River%2C_folio_from_a_Hindi_manuscript_by_the_saint_Narayan_LACMA_M.86.345.6.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shiva_Bearing_the_Descent_of_the_Ganges_River,_folio_from_a_Hindi_manuscript_by_the_saint_Narayan_LACMA_M.86.345.6.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva</a>, as <i>Gangadhara</i>,  bearing the Descent of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganges_river" title="Ganges river">Ganges</a>, as the goddess <a href="/wiki/Parvati" title="Parvati">Parvati</a>, the sage <a href="/wiki/Bhagiratha" title="Bhagiratha">Bhagiratha</a>, and the bull <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nandi_(bull)" title="Nandi (bull)">Nandi</a> look on (circa 1740).</div></div></div>
<p>It is <a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva</a>'s relationship with Ganga, that is the best-known in Ganges mythology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p147_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p147-86">[80]</a></sup>  Her descent, the <i>avatarana</i> is not a one-time event, but a continuously occurring one in which she is forever falling from heaven into his locks and being forever tamed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p147_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p147-86">[80]</a></sup>  Shiva, is depicted in Hindu iconography as <i>Gangadhara</i>, the "Bearer of the Ganga", with Ganga, shown as spout of water, rising from his hair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p147_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p147-86">[80]</a></sup>  The Shiva-Ganga relationship is both perpetual and intimate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p147_86-3"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p147-86">[80]</a></sup>  Shiva is sometimes called <i>Uma-Ganga-Patiswara</i> ("Husband and Lord of Uma (Parvati) and Ganga"), and Ganga often arouses the jealousy of Shiva's better-known consort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p147_86-4"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p147-86">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Ganga is the <i><a href="/wiki/Shakti" title="Shakti">shakti</a></i> or the moving, restless, rolling energy in the form of which the otherwise recluse and unapproachable Shiva appears on earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p219_84-4"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p219-84">[78]</a></sup>  As water, this moving energy can be felt, tasted, and absorbed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p219_84-5"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p219-84">[78]</a></sup>  The war-god <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Murugan" title="Murugan">Skanda</a> addresses the sage <a href="/wiki/Agastya" title="Agastya">Agastya</a> in the <i>Kashi Khand</i> of the <i><a href="/wiki/Skanda_Purana" title="Skanda Purana">Skanda Purana</a></i> in these words:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p219_84-6"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p219-84">[78]</a></sup> </p><blockquote><p>One should not be amazed ... that this Ganges is really Power, for is she not the Supreme Shakti of the Eternal Shiva, taken in the form of water?<br/>This Ganges, filled with the sweet wine of compassion, was sent out for the salvation of the world by Shiva, the Lord of the Lords.<br/>Good people should not think this Triple-Pathed River to be like the thousand other earthly rivers, filled with water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p219_84-7"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p219-84">[78]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The Ganga is also the mother, the <i>Ganga Mata</i> (<i>mata</i>="mother") of Hindu worship and culture, accepting all and forgiving all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p218_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p218-83">[77]</a></sup>  Unlike other goddesses, she has no destructive or fearsome aspect, destructive though she might be as a river in nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p218_83-3"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p218-83">[77]</a></sup>  She is also a mother to other gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p149_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p149-87">[81]</a></sup>  She accepts Shiva's incandescent seed from the fire-god <a href="/wiki/Agni" title="Agni">Agni</a>, which is too hot for this world and cools it in her waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p149_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p149-87">[81]</a></sup>  This union produces Skanda, or Kartikeya, the god of war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p149_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p149-87">[81]</a></sup>  In the <i><a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a></i>, she is the wife of <a href="/wiki/Shantanu" title="Shantanu">Shantanu</a>, and the mother of heroic warrior-patriarch, <a href="/wiki/Bhishma" title="Bhishma">Bhishma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p149_87-3"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p149-87">[81]</a></sup>  When Bhishma is mortally wounded in battle, Ganga comes out of the water in human form and weeps uncontrollably over his body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1998-p149_87-4"><a href="#cite_note-eck1998-p149-87">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Ganges is the distilled lifeblood of the Hindu tradition, of its divinities, holy books, and enlightenment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p219_84-8"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p219-84">[78]</a></sup>  As such, her worship does not require the usual rites of invocation (<i>avahana</i>) at the beginning and dismissal (<i>visarjana</i>) at the end, required in the worship of other gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p219_84-9"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p219-84">[78]</a></sup>  Her divinity is immediate and everlasting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eck1982-p219_84-10"><a href="#cite_note-eck1982-p219-84">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ganges_in_classical_Indian_iconography">Ganges in classical Indian iconography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ganges in classical Indian iconography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 165px"><div style="width: 165px">

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 165px"><div style="width: 165px">

<div class="gallerytext">
