</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul></div></div></div>
<p>Early in ancient Indian culture, the river Ganges was associated with fecundity, its redeeming waters, and its rich silt providing sustenance to all who lived along its banks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[82]</a></sup> A counterpoise to the dazzling heat of the Indian summer, the Ganges came to be imbued with magical qualities and to be revered in anthropomorphic form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[83]</a></sup> By the 5th century CE, an elaborate mythology surrounded the Ganges, now a goddess in her own right, and a symbol for all rivers of India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[84]</a></sup> Hindu temples all over India had statues and reliefs of the goddess carved at their entrances, symbolically washing the sins of arriving worshippers and guarding the gods within.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pal-1997-p43_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-pal-1997-p43-91">[85]</a></sup> As protector of the <a href="/wiki/Sanctum_sanctorum" title="Sanctum sanctorum">sanctum sanctorum</a>, the goddess soon came to be depicted with several characteristic accessories: the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Makara_(Hindu_mythology)" title="Makara (Hindu mythology)">makara</a></i> (a crocodile-like undersea monster, often shown with an elephant-like trunk), the <i><a href="/wiki/Kumbha" title="Kumbha">kumbha</a></i> (an overfull vase), various overhead parasol-like coverings, and a gradually increasing retinue of humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darian2001-p114_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-darian2001-p114-92">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>Central to the goddess's visual identification is the <i>makara</i>, which is also her <i><a href="/wiki/Vahana" title="Vahana">vahana</a></i>, or mount. An ancient symbol in India, it pre-dates all appearances of the goddess Ganga in art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darian2001-p114_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-darian2001-p114-92">[86]</a></sup> The <i>makara</i> has a dual symbolism. On the one hand, it represents the life-affirming waters and plants of its environment; on the other, it represents fear, both fear of the unknown which it elicits by lurking in those waters, and real fear which it instils by appearing in sight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darian2001-p114_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-darian2001-p114-92">[86]</a></sup> The earliest extant unambiguous pairing of the <i>makara</i> with Ganga is at the <a href="/wiki/Udayagiri_Caves" title="Udayagiri Caves">Udayagiri Caves</a> in Central India (circa 400 CE). Here, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Udaygiri_Caves#Caves,_sculptures_and_inscriptions" title="Udaygiri Caves">Cave V</a>, flanking the main figure of Vishnu shown in his boar incarnation, two river goddesses, Ganga and Yamuna appear atop their respective mounts, <i>makara</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Kurma" title="Kurma">kurma</a></i> (a turtle or tortoise).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darian2001-p114_92-3"><a href="#cite_note-darian2001-p114-92">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>makara</i> is often accompanied by a <i><a href="/wiki/Gana" title="Gana">gana</a></i>, a small boy or child, near its mouth, as, for example, shown in the Gupta period relief from <a href="/wiki/Vidisha#Historic_Places_and_Monuments" title="Vidisha">Besnagar</a>, Central India, in the left-most frame above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darian-2001-p118_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-darian-2001-p118-93">[87]</a></sup> The <i>gana</i> represents both posterity and development (<i>udbhava</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darian-2001-p118_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-darian-2001-p118-93">[87]</a></sup> The pairing of the fearsome, life-destroying <i>makara</i> with the youthful, life-affirming <i>gana</i> speaks to two aspects of the Ganges herself. Although she has provided sustenance to millions, she has also brought hardship, injury, and death by causing major floods along her banks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[88]</a></sup> The goddess Ganga is also accompanied by a dwarf attendant, who carries a cosmetic bag, and on whom she sometimes leans, as if for support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pal-1997-p43_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-pal-1997-p43-91">[85]</a></sup> (See, for example, frames 1, 2, and 4 above.)
</p><p>The <i>purna kumbha</i> or full pot of water is the second most discernible element of the Ganga iconography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darian-2001-p125_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-darian-2001-p125-95">[89]</a></sup> Appearing first also in the relief in the Udayagiri Caves (5th century), it gradually appeared more frequently as the theme of the goddess matured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darian-2001-p125_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-darian-2001-p125-95">[89]</a></sup> By the 7th century it had become an established feature, as seen, for example, in the <a href="/wiki/Deogarh,_Uttar_Pradesh#Dashavatara_temple" title="Deogarh, Uttar Pradesh">Dashavatara temple</a>, Deogarh, Uttar Pradesh (7th century), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Badoli_Temples" title="Badoli Temples">Trimurti temple</a>, <a href="/wiki/Badoli" title="Badoli">Badoli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chittor_Fort" title="Chittor Fort">Chittorgarh</a>, Rajasthan, and at the <a href="/wiki/Bilaspur,_Chhattisgarh#Tourist_attractions" title="Bilaspur, Chhattisgarh">Lakshmaneshwar temple</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kharod" title="Kharod">Kharod</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bilaspur,_Chhattisgarh" title="Bilaspur, Chhattisgarh">Bilaspur, Chhattisgarh</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darian-2001-p125_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-darian-2001-p125-95">[89]</a></sup> (9th or 10th century), and seen very clearly in frame 3 above and less clearly in the remaining frames. Worshipped even today, the full pot is emblematic of the formless <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a>, as well as of woman, of the womb, and of birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darian-2001-p126_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-darian-2001-p126-96">[90]</a></sup> Furthermore, The river goddesses Ganga and Saraswati were both born from Brahma's pot, containing the celestial waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darian-2001-p126_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-darian-2001-p126-96">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>In her earliest depictions at temple entrances, the goddess Ganga appeared standing beneath the overhanging branch of a tree, as seen as well in the Udayagiri caves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darian-2001-p130_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-darian-2001-p130-97">[91]</a></sup> However, soon the tree cover had evolved into a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chatra" title="Chatra">chatra</a></i> or parasol held by an attendant, for example, in the 7th-century Dasavatara temple at Deogarh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darian-2001-p130_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-darian-2001-p130-97">[91]</a></sup> (The parasol can be clearly seen in frame 3 above; its stem can be seen in frame 4, but the rest has broken off.) The cover undergoes another transformation in the temple at Kharod, Bilaspur (9th or 10th century), where the parasol is lotus-shaped,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darian-2001-p130_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-darian-2001-p130-97">[91]</a></sup> and yet another at the Trimurti temple at Badoli where the parasol has been replaced entirely by a lotus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darian-2001-p130_97-3"><a href="#cite_note-darian-2001-p130-97">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>
As the iconography evolved, sculptors, especially in central India, were producing animated scenes of the goddess, replete with an entourage and suggestive of a queen en route to a river to bathe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[92]</a></sup> A relief similar to the depiction in frame 4 above, is described in <a href="#CITEREFPal1997">Pal 1997</a>, p. 43 as follows: </p><blockquote><p> A typical relief of about the ninth century that once stood at the entrance of a temple, the river goddess Ganga is shown as a voluptuously endowed lady with a retinue. Following the iconographic prescription, she stands gracefully on her composite <i>makara</i> mount and holds a water pot. The dwarf attendant carries her cosmetic bag, and a ... female holds the stem of a giant lotus leaf that serves as her mistress's parasol. The fourth figure is a male guardian. Often in such reliefs, the <i>makara</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> tail is extended with great flourish into a scrolling design symbolizing both vegetation and water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pal-1997-p43_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-pal-1997-p43-91">[85]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kumbh_Mela">Kumbh Mela</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Kumbh Mela">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kumbh_Mela2001.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3509" data-file-width="5088" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Kumbh_Mela2001.JPG/220px-Kumbh_Mela2001.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Kumbh_Mela2001.JPG/330px-Kumbh_Mela2001.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Kumbh_Mela2001.JPG/440px-Kumbh_Mela2001.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kumbh_Mela2001.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A procession of <a href="/wiki/Akhara" title="Akhara">Akharas</a> marching over a makeshift bridge over the Ganges River. Kumbh Mela at <a href="/wiki/Allahabad" title="Allahabad">Allahabad</a>, 2001.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kumbh_Mela" title="Kumbh Mela">Kumbh Mela</a></div>
<p>Kumbh Mela is a mass Hindu <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">pilgrimage</a> in which Hindus gather at the Ganges River. The normal Kumbh <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mela" title="Mela">Mela</a> is celebrated every 3 years, the <i>Ardh</i> (half) Kumbh is celebrated every six years at Haridwar and <a href="/wiki/Allahabad" title="Allahabad">Allahabad</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[93]</a></sup> the <i>Purna</i> (complete) Kumbh takes place every twelve years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hydro_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-hydro-100">[94]</a></sup> at four places (<a href="/wiki/Triveni_Sangam" title="Triveni Sangam">Triveni Sangam</a> (Allahabad), Haridwar, <a href="/wiki/Ujjain" title="Ujjain">Ujjain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nashik" title="Nashik">Nashik</a>). The <i>Maha</i> (great) Kumbh Mela which comes after 12 'Purna Kumbh Melas', or 144 years, is held at Allahabad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hydro_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-hydro-100">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>The major event of the festival is <a href="/wiki/Ritual_purification" title="Ritual purification">ritual bathing</a> at the banks of the river. Other activities include religious discussions, devotional singing, mass feeding of holy men and women and the poor, and religious assemblies where doctrines are debated and standardized. Kumbh Mela is the most sacred of all the pilgrimages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[96]</a></sup> Thousands of holy men and women attend, and the auspiciousness of the festival is in part attributable to this. The <a href="/wiki/Sadhu" title="Sadhu">sadhus</a> are seen clad in saffron sheets with ashes and powder dabbed on their skin per the requirements of ancient traditions. Some called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dashanami_Sampradaya#Naga_Sadhus" title="Dashanami Sampradaya">naga sanyasis</a></i>, may not wear any clothes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Irrigation">Irrigation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Irrigation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Ganges and its all tributaries, especially the Yamuna, have been used for irrigation since ancient times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh200569–79_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh200569–79-105">[99]</a></sup> Dams and canals were common in the Gangetic plain by the 4th century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHill2008_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHill2008-106">[100]</a></sup> The Ganges-Brahmaputra-Meghna basin has a huge <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectric</a> potential, on the order of 200,000 to 250,000 megawatts, nearly half of which could easily be harnessed. As of 1999, India tapped about 12% of the hydroelectric potential of the Ganges and just 1% of the vast potential of the Brahmaputra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElhance1999163_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElhance1999163-107">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canals">Canals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Canals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Headworks_ganges_canal_haridwar1860.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="525" data-file-width="976" decoding="async" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a5/Headworks_ganges_canal_haridwar1860.jpg/220px-Headworks_ganges_canal_haridwar1860.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a5/Headworks_ganges_canal_haridwar1860.jpg/330px-Headworks_ganges_canal_haridwar1860.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a5/Headworks_ganges_canal_haridwar1860.jpg/440px-Headworks_ganges_canal_haridwar1860.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Headworks_ganges_canal_haridwar1860.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Head works of the Ganges canal in Haridwar (1860). Photograph by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Bourne" title="Samuel Bourne">Samuel Bourne</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Megasthenes, a Greek ethnographer who visited India during the 3rd century BCE when Mauryans ruled India described the existence of canals in the Gangetic plain. Kautilya (also known as <a href="/wiki/Chanakya" title="Chanakya">Chanakya</a>), an advisor to <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_Maurya" title="Chandragupta Maurya">Chandragupta Maurya</a>, the founder of <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Maurya Empire</a>, included the destruction of dams and levees as a strategy during the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHill2008_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHill2008-106">[100]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Firuz_Shah_Tughlaq" title="Firuz Shah Tughlaq">Firuz Shah Tughlaq</a> had many canals built, the longest of which, 240 km (150 mi), was built in 1356 on the Yamuna River. Now known as the Western Yamuna Canal, it has fallen into disrepair and been restored several times. The <a href="/wiki/Mughal_emperors" title="Mughal emperors">Mughal emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a> built an irrigation canal on the Yamuna River in the early 17th century. It fell into disuse until 1830, when it was reopened as the Eastern Yamuna Canal, under British control. The reopened canal became a model for the Upper Ganges Canal and all following canal projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh200569–79_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh200569–79-105">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GangesCanal2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1777" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="326" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2b/GangesCanal2.jpg/220px-GangesCanal2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2b/GangesCanal2.jpg/330px-GangesCanal2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2b/GangesCanal2.jpg/440px-GangesCanal2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GangesCanal2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Ganges Canal highlighted in red stretching between its headworks off the Ganges River in Haridwar and its confluences with the Jumna (Yamuna) River in <a href="/wiki/Etawah" title="Etawah">Etawah</a> and with the Ganges in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cawnpore" title="Cawnpore">Cawnpore</a> (now Kanpur).</div></div></div>
<p>The first British canal in India (which did not have Indian antecedents) was the <a href="/wiki/Ganges_Canal" title="Ganges Canal">Ganges Canal</a> built between 1842 and 1854.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stone2002-p16_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-stone2002-p16-108">[102]</a></sup>
Contemplated first by Col. <a href="/wiki/John_Russell_Colvin" title="John Russell Colvin">John Russell Colvin</a> in 1836, it did not at first elicit much enthusiasm from its eventual architect Sir <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proby_Thomas_Cautley" title="Proby Thomas Cautley">Proby Thomas Cautley</a>, who balked at the idea of cutting a canal through extensive low-lying land to reach the drier upland destination. However, after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agra_famine_of_1837%E2%80%9338" title="Agra famine of 1837–38">Agra famine of 1837–38</a>, during which the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a>'s administration spent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rs." title="Rs.">Rs.</a> 2,300,000 on famine relief, the idea of a canal became more attractive to the company's budget-conscious Court of Directors. In 1839, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Governor_General_of_India" title="Governor General of India">Governor General of India</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Eden,_1st_Earl_of_Auckland" title="George Eden, 1st Earl of Auckland">Lord Auckland</a>, with the Court's assent, granted funds to Cautley for a full survey of the swath of land that underlay and fringed the projected course of the canal. The Court of Directors, moreover, considerably enlarged the scope of the projected canal, which, in consequence of the severity and geographical extent of the famine, they now deemed to be the entire <a href="/wiki/Doab" title="Doab">Doab</a> region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>
The enthusiasm, however, proved to be short-lived. Auckland's successor as Governor-General, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Law,_1st_Earl_of_Ellenborough" title="Edward Law, 1st Earl of Ellenborough">Lord Ellenborough</a>, appeared less receptive to large-scale public works, and for the duration of his tenure, withheld major funds for the project. Only in 1844, when a new Governor-General, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Hardinge,_1st_Viscount_Hardinge" title="Henry Hardinge, 1st Viscount Hardinge">Lord Hardinge</a>, was appointed, did official enthusiasm and funds return to the Ganges canal project. Although the intervening impasse had seemingly affected Cautley's health and required him to return to Britain in 1845 for recuperation, his European sojourn gave him an opportunity to study contemporary hydraulic works in the United Kingdom and Italy. By the time of his return to India even more supportive men were at the helm, both in the <a href="/wiki/North-Western_Provinces" title="North-Western Provinces">North-Western Provinces</a>, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Thomason_(British_colonial_governor)" title="James Thomason (British colonial governor)">James Thomason</a> as Lt. Governor, and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_India" title="British India">British India</a> with <a href="/wiki/James_Broun-Ramsay,_1st_Marquess_of_Dalhousie" title="James Broun-Ramsay, 1st Marquess of Dalhousie">Lord Dalhousie</a> as Governor-General. Canal construction, under Cautley's supervision, now went into full swing. A 560 km (350 mi) long canal, with another 480 km (300 mi) of branch lines, eventually stretched between the headworks in Haridwar, splitting into two branches below <a href="/wiki/Aligarh" title="Aligarh">Aligarh</a>, and its two confluences with the Yamuna (Jumna in map) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mainstem_(hydrology)" title="Mainstem (hydrology)">mainstem</a> in <a href="/wiki/Etawah" title="Etawah">Etawah</a> and the Ganges in Kanpur (Cawnpore in map). The Ganges Canal, which required a total capital outlay of £2.15 million, was officially opened in 1854 by Lord Dalhousie.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrakash1999162_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrakash1999162-110">[104]</a></sup> According to historian Ian Stone: </p><blockquote><p>It was the largest canal ever attempted in the world, five times greater in its length than all the main irrigation lines of <a href="/wiki/Lombardy" title="Lombardy">Lombardy</a> and Egypt put together, and longer by a third than even the largest USA navigation canal, the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Canal" title="Pennsylvania Canal">Pennsylvania Canal</a>.</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dams_and_barrages">Dams and barrages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Dams and barrages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A major <a href="/wiki/Barrage_(dam)" title="Barrage (dam)">barrage</a> at Farakka was opened on 21 April 1975,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrichieri-ColombiBradnock2003_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrichieri-ColombiBradnock2003-111">[105]</a></sup> It is located close to the point where the main flow of the river enters Bangladesh, and the tributary Hooghly (also known as Bhagirathi) continues in West Bengal past
Kolkata. This barrage, which feeds the Hooghly branch of the river by a 42 km (26 mi) long feeder canal, and its water flow management has been a long-lingering source of dispute with Bangladesh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[106]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharing_the_water_of_the_Ganges" title="Sharing the water of the Ganges">Indo-Bangladesh Ganges Water Treaty</a> signed in December 1996 addressed some of the water sharing issues between India and Bangladesh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrichieri-ColombiBradnock2003_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrichieri-ColombiBradnock2003-111">[105]</a></sup> There is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lav_Khush_Barrage" title="Lav Khush Barrage">Lav Khush Barrage</a> across the River Ganges in Kanpur.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tehri_Dam" title="Tehri Dam">Tehri Dam</a> was constructed on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bhagirathi" title="Bhagirathi">Bhagirathi</a> River, a tributary of the Ganges. It is located 1.5 km downstream of Ganesh Prayag, the place where Bhilangana meets Bhagirathi. Bhagirathi is called the Ganges after Devprayag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharmaBahugunaChauhan2008_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharmaBahugunaChauhan2008-113">[107]</a></sup> Construction of the dam in an earthquake-prone area<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrune1993_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrune1993-114">[108]</a></sup> was controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bansagar_Dam" title="Bansagar Dam">Bansagar Dam</a> was built on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sone_River" title="Sone River">Sone River</a>, a tributary of the Ganges for both irrigation and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroelectric" title="Hydroelectric">hydroelectric</a> power generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[110]</a></sup> Ganges floodwaters along with Brahmaputra waters can be supplied to most of its right side basin area along with central and south India by constructing a <a href="/wiki/Coastal_reservoir" title="Coastal reservoir">coastal reservoir</a> to store water on the Bay of Bengal sea area.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_girl_selling_plastic_containers_for_carrying_Ganges_water,_Haridwar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/A_girl_selling_plastic_containers_for_carrying_Ganges_water%2C_Haridwar.jpg/170px-A_girl_selling_plastic_containers_for_carrying_Ganges_water%2C_Haridwar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/A_girl_selling_plastic_containers_for_carrying_Ganges_water%2C_Haridwar.jpg/255px-A_girl_selling_plastic_containers_for_carrying_Ganges_water%2C_Haridwar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/A_girl_selling_plastic_containers_for_carrying_Ganges_water%2C_Haridwar.jpg/340px-A_girl_selling_plastic_containers_for_carrying_Ganges_water%2C_Haridwar.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_girl_selling_plastic_containers_for_carrying_Ganges_water,_Haridwar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A girl selling plastic containers in Haridwar for carrying Ganges water.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ganges_Basin" title="Ganges Basin">Ganges Basin</a> with its fertile soil is instrumental to the agricultural economies of India and Bangladesh. The Ganges and its tributaries provide a perennial source of irrigation to a large area. Chief crops cultivated in the area include rice, <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lentil" title="Lentil">lentils</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_seed" title="Oil seed">oil seeds</a>, potatoes, and wheat. Along the banks of the river, the presence of <a href="/wiki/Swamp" title="Swamp">swamps</a> and lakes provides a rich growing area for crops such as legumes, chillies, mustard, sesame, sugarcane, and jute. There are also many fishing opportunities along the river, though it remains highly polluted. Also, the major industrial towns of <a href="/wiki/Unnao" title="Unnao">Unnao</a> and Kanpur, situated on the banks of the river with the predominance of tanning industries add to the pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Tourism is another related activity. Three towns holy to Hinduism—Haridwar, Allahabad (Prayagraj), and Varanasi—attract millions of pilgrims to its waters to take a dip in the Ganges, which is believed to cleanse oneself of sins and help attain salvation. The rapids of the Ganges are also popular for <a href="/wiki/Rafting" title="Rafting">river rafting</a> in the town of Rishikesh, attracting adventure seekers in the summer months. Several cities such as Kanpur, Kolkata and Patna have also developed riverfront walkways along the banks to attract tourists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Behteri1_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Behteri1-118">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Behteri3_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Behteri3-119">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Behteri2_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Behteri2-120">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology_and_environment">Ecology and environment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Ecology and environment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bangladesh_tmo_2011313.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Bangladesh_tmo_2011313.jpg/220px-Bangladesh_tmo_2011313.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Bangladesh_tmo_2011313.jpg/330px-Bangladesh_tmo_2011313.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Bangladesh_tmo_2011313.jpg/440px-Bangladesh_tmo_2011313.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bangladesh_tmo_2011313.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Ganges from Space</i></div></div></div>
<p>Human development, mostly agriculture, has replaced nearly all of the original natural vegetation of the Ganges basin. More than 95% of the upper Gangetic Plain has been degraded or converted to agriculture or urban areas. Only one large block of relatively intact habitat remains, running along the Himalayan foothills and including <a href="/wiki/Rajaji_National_Park" title="Rajaji National Park">Rajaji National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jim_Corbett_National_Park" title="Jim Corbett National Park">Jim Corbett National Park</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dudhwa_National_Park" title="Dudhwa National Park">Dudhwa National Park</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwf0166_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-wwf0166-122">[116]</a></sup> As recently as the 16th and 17th centuries the upper Gangetic Plain harboured impressive populations of wild <a href="/wiki/Asian_elephant" title="Asian elephant">Asian elephants</a> (<i>Elephas maximus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Bengal_tiger" title="Bengal tiger">Bengal tigers</a> (<i>Panthera t. tigris</i>), <a href="/wiki/Indian_rhinoceros" title="Indian rhinoceros">Indian rhinoceros</a> (<i>Rhinoceros unicornis</i>), <a href="/wiki/Gaur" title="Gaur">gaurs</a> (<i>Bos gaurus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Barasingha" title="Barasingha">barasinghas</a> (<i>Rucervus duvaucelii</i>), <a href="/wiki/Sloth_bear" title="Sloth bear">sloth bears</a> (<i>Melursus ursinus</i>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_lion" title="Indian lion">Indian lions</a> (<i>Panthera leo leo</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwf0166_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-wwf0166-122">[116]</a></sup> In the 21st century there are few large wild animals, mostly deer, <a href="/wiki/Wild_boar" title="Wild boar">wild boars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wildcat" title="Wildcat">wildcats</a>, and small numbers of <a href="/wiki/Indian_wolf" title="Indian wolf">Indian wolves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Golden_jackal" title="Golden jackal">golden jackals</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">red</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bengal_fox" title="Bengal fox">Bengal foxes</a>. Bengal tigers survive only in the <a href="/wiki/Sundarbans" title="Sundarbans">Sundarbans</a> area of the Ganges Delta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-24">[19]</a></sup> The Sundarbands freshwater swamp ecoregion, however, is nearly extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwf0162_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-wwf0162-123">[117]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Heritiera_fomes" title="Heritiera fomes">Sundarbans mangroves</a> (<i>Heritiera fomes</i>) also grow in the Sundarbans area of the Ganges Delta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[118]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Threatened_species" title="Threatened species">Threatened</a> mammals in the upper Gangetic Plain include the tiger, elephant, sloth bear, and <a href="/wiki/Four-horned_antelope" title="Four-horned antelope">four-horned antelope</a> (<i>Tetracerus quadricornis</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwf0166_122-2"><a href="#cite_note-wwf0166-122">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Florican,_1781.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1747" data-file-width="1327" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Florican%2C_1781.jpg/170px-Florican%2C_1781.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Florican%2C_1781.jpg/255px-Florican%2C_1781.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Florican%2C_1781.jpg/340px-Florican%2C_1781.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Florican,_1781.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lesser_florican" title="Lesser florican">Lesser florican</a> (<i>Sypheotides indicus</i>)</div></div></div>
<p>Many types of birds are found throughout the basin, such as <a href="/wiki/Myna" title="Myna">myna</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Psittacula" title="Psittacula">Psittacula</a></i> parakeets, <a href="/wiki/Crow" title="Crow">crows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kite_(bird)" title="Kite (bird)">kites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Partridge" title="Partridge">partridges</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fowl" title="Fowl">fowls</a>. <a href="/wiki/Duck" title="Duck">Ducks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Snipe" title="Snipe">snipes</a> migrate across the Himalayas during the winter, attracted in large numbers to wetland areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-24">[19]</a></sup> There are no <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> birds in the upper Gangetic Plain. The <a href="/wiki/Great_Indian_bustard" title="Great Indian bustard">great Indian bustard</a> (<i>Ardeotis nigriceps</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Lesser_florican" title="Lesser florican">lesser florican</a> (<i>Sypheotides indicus</i>) are considered globally threatened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwf0166_122-3"><a href="#cite_note-wwf0166-122">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>The natural forest of the upper Gangetic Plain has been so thoroughly eliminated it is difficult to assign a natural vegetation type with certainty. There are a few small patches of forest left, and they suggest that much of the upper plains may have supported a <a href="/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_moist_broadleaf_forests" title="Tropical and subtropical moist broadleaf forests">tropical moist deciduous forest</a> with sal (<i><a href="/wiki/Shorea_robusta" title="Shorea robusta">Shorea robusta</a></i>) as a <a href="/wiki/Climax_species" title="Climax species">climax species</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwf0166_122-4"><a href="#cite_note-wwf0166-122">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>A similar situation is found in the lower Gangetic Plain, which includes the lower Brahmaputra River. The lower plains contain more open forests, which tend to be dominated by <i><a href="/wiki/Bombax_ceiba" title="Bombax ceiba">Bombax ceiba</a></i> in association with <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Albizzia_procera&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Albizzia procera (page does not exist)">Albizzia procera</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Duabanga_grandiflora" title="Duabanga grandiflora">Duabanga grandiflora</a></i>, and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sterculia_vilosa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sterculia vilosa (page does not exist)">Sterculia vilosa</a></i>. There are early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seral" title="Seral">seral</a> forest communities that would eventually become dominated by the climax species sal (<i>Shorea robusta</i>) if forest succession was allowed to proceed. In most places forests fail to reach climax conditions due to human causes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwf0120_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-wwf0120-125">[119]</a></sup> The forests of the lower Gangetic Plain, despite thousands of years of human settlement, remained largely intact until the early 20th century. Today only about 3% of the ecoregion is under natural forest and only one large block, south of Varanasi, remains. There are over forty protected areas in the ecoregion, but over half of these are less than 100 square kilometres (39 sq mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwf0120_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-wwf0120-125">[119]</a></sup> The fauna of the lower Gangetic Plain is similar to the upper plains, with the addition of a number of other species such as the <a href="/wiki/Smooth-coated_otter" title="Smooth-coated otter">smooth-coated otter</a> (<i>Lutrogale perspicillata</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Large_Indian_civet" title="Large Indian civet">large Indian civet</a> (<i>Viverra zibetha</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwf0120_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-wwf0120-125">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fish">Fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Catla_catla_India.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Catla_catla_India.jpg/220px-Catla_catla_India.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Catla_catla_India.jpg/330px-Catla_catla_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Catla_catla_India.jpg/440px-Catla_catla_India.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Catla_catla_India.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Catla" title="Catla">catla</a> (<i>Catla catla</i>) is one of the Indian carp species that support major fisheries in the Ganges</div></div></div>
<p>It has been estimated that about 350 fish species live in the entire Ganges drainage, including several <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[120]</a></sup> In a major 2007–2009 study of fish in the Ganges basin (including the river itself and its tributaries, but excluding the Brahmaputra and Meghna basins), a total of 143 fish species were recorded, including 10 non-native <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">introduced species</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarkar2012_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sarkar2012-127">[121]</a></sup> The most diverse orders are <a href="/wiki/Cypriniformes" title="Cypriniformes">Cypriniformes</a> (barbs and allies), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siluriformes" title="Siluriformes">Siluriformes</a> (catfish) and <a href="/wiki/Perciformes" title="Perciformes">Perciformes</a> (perciform fish), each comprising about 50%, 23% and 14% of the total fish species in the drainage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarkar2012_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sarkar2012-127">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are distinct differences between the different sections of the river basin, but <a href="/wiki/Cyprinidae" title="Cyprinidae">Cyprinidae</a> is the most diverse throughout. In the upper section (roughly equalling the basin parts in Uttarakhand) more than 50 species have been recorded and Cyprinidae alone accounts for almost 80% those, followed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balitoridae" title="Balitoridae">Balitoridae</a> (about 15.6%) and <a href="/wiki/Sisoridae" title="Sisoridae">Sisoridae</a> (about 12.2%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarkar2012_127-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sarkar2012-127">[121]</a></sup> Sections of the Ganges basin at altitudes above 2,400–3,000 m (7,900–9,800 ft) above sea level are generally without fish. Typical genera approaching this altitude are <i><a href="/wiki/Schizothorax" title="Schizothorax">Schizothorax</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tor_(genus)" title="Tor (genus)">Tor</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Barilius" title="Barilius">Barilius</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Nemacheilus" title="Nemacheilus">Nemacheilus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Glyptothorax" title="Glyptothorax">Glyptothorax</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarkar2012_127-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sarkar2012-127">[121]</a></sup> About 100 species have been recorded from the middle section of the basin (roughly equalling the sections in Uttar Pradesh and parts of Bihar) and more than 55% of these are in family Cyprinidae, followed by <a href="/wiki/Schilbeidae" title="Schilbeidae">Schilbeidae</a> (about 10.6%) and <a href="/wiki/Clupeidae" title="Clupeidae">Clupeidae</a> (about 8.6%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarkar2012_127-4"><a href="#cite_note-Sarkar2012-127">[121]</a></sup> The lower section (roughly equalling the basin in parts of Bihar and West Bengal) includes major floodplains and is home to almost 100 species. About 46% of these are in the family Cyprinidae, followed by Schilbeidae (about 11.4%) and <a href="/wiki/Bagridae" title="Bagridae">Bagridae</a> (about 9%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarkar2012_127-5"><a href="#cite_note-Sarkar2012-127">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Ganges basin supports major fisheries, but these have declined in recent decades. In the <a href="/wiki/Allahabad" title="Allahabad">Allahabad</a> region in the middle section of the basin, catches of carp fell from 424.91 metric tons in 1961–1968 to 38.58 metric tons in 2001–2006, and catches of catfish fell from 201.35 metric tons in 1961–1968 to 40.56 metric tons in 2001–2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarkar2012_127-6"><a href="#cite_note-Sarkar2012-127">[121]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Patna" title="Patna">Patna</a> region in the lower section of the basin, catches of carp fell from 383.2 metric tons to 118, and catfish from 373.8 metric tons to 194.48.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarkar2012_127-7"><a href="#cite_note-Sarkar2012-127">[121]</a></sup> Some of the fish commonly caught in fisheries include <a href="/wiki/Catla" title="Catla">catla</a> (<i>Catla catla</i>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_mahseer" title="Golden mahseer">golden mahseer</a> (<i>Tor putitora</i>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tor_mahseer" title="Tor mahseer">tor mahseer</a> (<i>Tor tor</i>), <a href="/wiki/Rohu" title="Rohu">rohu</a> (<i>Labeo rohita</i>), <a href="/wiki/Walking_catfish" title="Walking catfish">walking catfish</a> (<i>Clarias batrachus</i>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pangas_catfish" title="Pangas catfish">pangas catfish</a> (<i>Pangasius pangasius</i>), <a href="/wiki/Bagarius" title="Bagarius">goonch catfish</a> (<i>Bagarius</i>), <a href="/wiki/Snakehead_(fish)" title="Snakehead (fish)">snakeheads</a> (<i>Channa</i>), <a href="/wiki/Bronze_featherback" title="Bronze featherback">bronze featherback</a> (<i>Notopterus notopterus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Milkfish" title="Milkfish">milkfish</a> (<i>Chanos chanos</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_24-5"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-24">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarkar2012_127-8"><a href="#cite_note-Sarkar2012-127">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Ganges basin is home to about 30 fish species that are listed as threatened with the primary issues being <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a> (sometimes illegal), pollution, water abstraction, <a href="/wiki/Siltation" title="Siltation">siltation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive species</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarkar2012_127-9"><a href="#cite_note-Sarkar2012-127">[121]</a></sup> Among the threatened species is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critically_endangered" title="Critically endangered">critically endangered</a> <a href="/wiki/Ganges_shark" title="Ganges shark">Ganges shark</a> (<i>Glyphis gangeticus</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[122]</a></sup> Several fish species <a href="/wiki/Fish_migration" title="Fish migration">migrate</a> between different sections of the river, but these movements may be prevented by the building of dams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarkar2012_127-10"><a href="#cite_note-Sarkar2012-127">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crocodilians_and_turtles">Crocodilians and turtles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Crocodilians and turtles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gavialis_gangeticus,_ZOO_Praha_045.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="3750" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Gavialis_gangeticus%2C_ZOO_Praha_045.jpg/220px-Gavialis_gangeticus%2C_ZOO_Praha_045.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Gavialis_gangeticus%2C_ZOO_Praha_045.jpg/330px-Gavialis_gangeticus%2C_ZOO_Praha_045.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Gavialis_gangeticus%2C_ZOO_Praha_045.jpg/440px-Gavialis_gangeticus%2C_ZOO_Praha_045.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gavialis_gangeticus,_ZOO_Praha_045.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The threatened <a href="/wiki/Gharial" title="Gharial">gharial</a> (<i>Gavialis gangeticus</i>) is a large fish-eating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crocodilian" title="Crocodilian">crocodilian</a> that is harmless to humans<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[123]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The main sections of the Ganges River are home to the <a href="/wiki/Gharial" title="Gharial">gharial</a> (<i>Gavialis gangeticus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Mugger_crocodile" title="Mugger crocodile">mugger crocodile</a> (<i>Crocodylus palustris</i>), and the Ganges delta is home to the <a href="/wiki/Saltwater_crocodile" title="Saltwater crocodile">saltwater crocodile</a> (<i>C. porosus</i>). Among the numerous aquatic and semi-aquatic turtles in the Ganges basin are the <a href="/wiki/Northern_river_terrapin" title="Northern river terrapin">northern river terrapin</a> (<i>Batagur baska</i>; only in the lowermost section of the basin), <a href="/wiki/Three-striped_roofed_turtle" title="Three-striped roofed turtle">three-striped roofed turtle</a> (<i>B. dhongoka</i>), <a href="/wiki/Red-crowned_roofed_turtle" title="Red-crowned roofed turtle">red-crowned roofed turtle</a> (<i>B. kachuga</i>), <a href="/wiki/Black_pond_turtle" title="Black pond turtle">black pond turtle</a> (<i>Geoclemys hamiltonii</i>), <a href="/wiki/Brahminy_river_turtle" title="Brahminy river turtle">Brahminy river turtle</a> (<i>Hardella thurjii</i>), <a href="/wiki/Indian_black_turtle" title="Indian black turtle">Indian black turtle</a> (<i>Melanochelys trijuga</i>), <a href="/wiki/Indian_eyed_turtle" title="Indian eyed turtle">Indian eyed turtle</a> (<i>Morenia petersi</i>), <a href="/wiki/Brown_roofed_turtle" title="Brown roofed turtle">brown roofed turtle</a> (<i>Pangshura smithii</i>), <a href="/wiki/Indian_roofed_turtle" title="Indian roofed turtle">Indian roofed turtle</a> (<i>Pangshura tecta</i>), <a href="/wiki/Indian_tent_turtle" title="Indian tent turtle">Indian tent turtle</a> (<i>Pangshura tentoria</i>), <a href="/wiki/Indian_flapshell_turtle" title="Indian flapshell turtle">Indian flapshell turtle</a> (<i>Lissemys punctata</i>), <a href="/wiki/Indian_narrow-headed_softshell_turtle" title="Indian narrow-headed softshell turtle">Indian narrow-headed softshell turtle</a> (<i>Chitra indica</i>), <a href="/wiki/Indian_softshell_turtle" title="Indian softshell turtle">Indian softshell turtle</a> (<i>Nilssonia gangetica</i>), <a href="/wiki/Indian_peacock_softshell_turtle" title="Indian peacock softshell turtle">Indian peacock softshell turtle</a> (<i>N. hurum</i>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cantor%27s_giant_softshell_turtle" title="Cantor's giant softshell turtle">Cantor's giant softshell turtle</a> (<i>Pelochelys cantorii</i>; only in the lowermost section of Ganges basin).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turtles_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-turtles-130">[124]</a></sup> Most of these are seriously threatened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turtles_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-turtles-130">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ganges_river_dolphin">Ganges river dolphin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Ganges river dolphin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GangeticDolphin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="557" data-file-width="921" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/GangeticDolphin.jpg/220px-GangeticDolphin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/GangeticDolphin.jpg/330px-GangeticDolphin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/GangeticDolphin.jpg/440px-GangeticDolphin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GangeticDolphin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Gangetic dolphin in a sketch by Whymper and P. Smit, 1894.</div></div></div>
<p>The river's most famed faunal member is the freshwater <a href="/wiki/Ganges_river_dolphin" title="Ganges river dolphin">Ganges river dolphin</a> (<i>Platanista gangetica gangetica</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwf0166_122-5"><a href="#cite_note-wwf0166-122">[116]</a></sup> which has been declared India's <a href="/wiki/List_of_national_animals" title="List of national animals">national aquatic animal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>This dolphin used to exist in large schools near urban centres in both the Ganges and Brahmaputra rivers but is now seriously threatened by pollution and dam construction. Their numbers have now dwindled to a quarter of their numbers of fifteen years before, and they have become extinct in the Ganges' main tributaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-puttick_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-puttick-23">[e]</a></sup> A recent survey by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Wildlife_Fund" title="World Wildlife Fund">World Wildlife Fund</a> found only 3,000 left in the water catchment of both river systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Ganges river dolphin is one of only five true <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater_dolphin" title="Freshwater dolphin">freshwater dolphins</a> in the world. The other four are the <a href="/wiki/Baiji" title="Baiji">baiji</a> (<i>Lipotes vexillifer</i>) of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze River</a> in China, now likely extinct; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_River_dolphin" title="Indus River dolphin">Indus River dolphin</a> of the Indus River in Pakistan; the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_river_dolphin" title="Amazon river dolphin">Amazon river dolphin</a> of the Amazon River in South America; and the <a href="/wiki/Araguaian_river_dolphin" title="Araguaian river dolphin">Araguaian river dolphin</a> (not considered a separate species until 2014<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[127]</a></sup>) of the <a href="/wiki/Tocantins_River" title="Tocantins River">Araguaia–Tocantins</a> basin in Brazil. There are several marine dolphins whose ranges include some freshwater habitats, but these five are the only dolphins who live only in freshwater rivers and lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwf0120_125-3"><a href="#cite_note-wwf0120-125">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_climate_change">Effects of climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Effects of climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Plateau" title="Tibetan Plateau">Tibetan Plateau</a> contains the world's third-largest store of ice. Qin Dahe, the former head of the China Meteorological Administration, said that the recent fast pace of melting and warmer temperatures will be good for agriculture and tourism in the short term; but issued a strong warning:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Temperatures are rising four times faster than elsewhere in China, and the Tibetan glaciers are retreating at a higher speed than in any other part of the world. ... In the short term, this will cause lakes to expand and bring floods and mudflows ... In the long run, the glaciers are vital lifelines for Asian rivers, including the Indus and the Ganges. Once they vanish, water supplies in those regions will be in peril.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[128]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In 2007, the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC), in its Fourth Report, stated that the Himalayan glaciers which feed the river were at risk of melting by 2035.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[129]</a></sup> The IPCC has now withdrawn that prediction, as the original source admitted that it was speculative and the cited source was not a peer-reviewed finding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[g]</a></sup> In its statement, the IPCC stands by its general findings relating to the Himalayan glaciers being at risk from global warming (with consequent risks to water flow into the Gangetic basin). Many studies have suggested that climate change will affect the water resources in the Ganges river basin including increased summer (monsoon) flow, and peak runoff could result in an increased risk of flooding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollution_and_environmental_concerns">Pollution and environmental concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Pollution and environmental concerns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pollution_of_the_Ganges" title="Pollution of the Ganges">Pollution of the Ganges</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cremation_in_Varanasi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="658" data-file-width="1032" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Cremation_in_Varanasi.jpg/250px-Cremation_in_Varanasi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Cremation_in_Varanasi.jpg/375px-Cremation_in_Varanasi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Cremation_in_Varanasi.jpg/500px-Cremation_in_Varanasi.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cremation_in_Varanasi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burning <i><a href="/wiki/Ghats_in_Varanasi" title="Ghats in Varanasi">ghats</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Varanasi" title="Varanasi">Varanasi</a>; the ashes of the dead are released along the banks of the Ganges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natgeo_2014_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Natgeo_2014-138">[131]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clothing_by_the_river.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Clothing_by_the_river.jpg/250px-Clothing_by_the_river.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Clothing_by_the_river.jpg/375px-Clothing_by_the_river.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Clothing_by_the_river.jpg/500px-Clothing_by_the_river.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clothing_by_the_river.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>People bathing and washing clothes along the banks of the Ganges in Varanasi</div></div></div>
<p>The Ganges suffers from extreme pollution levels,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[132]</a></sup> caused by the 400 million people who live close to the river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[134]</a></sup> Sewage from many cities along the river's course, industrial waste and religious offerings wrapped in non-degradable plastics add large amounts of pollutants to the river as it flows through densely populated areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cleanperish_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-cleanperish-20">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbraham2011_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbraham2011-142">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Behteri4_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Behteri4-143">[136]</a></sup> The problem is exacerbated by the fact that many poorer people rely on the river on a daily basis for bathing, washing, and cooking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbraham2011_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbraham2011-142">[135]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> estimates that the health costs of <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution_in_India" title="Water pollution in India">water pollution in India</a> equal three percent of India's GDP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bharati_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-bharati-144">[h]</a></sup> It has also been suggested that eighty percent of all illnesses in India and one-third of deaths can be attributed to water-borne diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-puttick_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-puttick-23">[e]</a></sup>
</p><p>Varanasi, a city of one million people that many pilgrims visit to take a "holy dip" in the Ganges, releases around 200 million liters of untreated human sewage into the river each day, leading to large concentrations of <a href="/wiki/Fecal_coliform" title="Fecal coliform">fecal coliform</a> bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbraham2011_142-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbraham2011-142">[135]</a></sup> According to official standards, water safe for bathing should not contain more than 500 fecal coliforms per 100 ml, yet upstream of <a href="/wiki/Ghats_in_Varanasi" title="Ghats in Varanasi">Varanasi's ghats</a> the river water already contains 120 times as much, 60,000 fecal coliform bacteria per 100 ml.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist2008-ganges-pollution_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-economist2008-ganges-pollution-145">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nomoreabuse_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-nomoreabuse-146">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Cremation" title="Cremation">cremation</a> of the deceased at Varanasi's ghats, the bones and ashes are immersed into the Ganges. However, in the past thousands of uncremated bodies were thrown into the Ganges during <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> epidemics, spreading the disease. Even today, holy men, pregnant women, people with <a href="/wiki/Leprosy" title="Leprosy">leprosy</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicken_pox" title="Chicken pox">chicken pox</a>, people who have been bitten by snakes, people who have committed suicide, the poor, and children under 5 are not cremated at the ghats but are left to float free, to decompose in the waters. In addition, those who cannot afford the large amount of wood needed to incinerate the entire body, leave behind a lot of half-burned body parts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>After passing through Varanasi, and receiving 32 streams of raw sewage from the city, the concentration of fecal coliforms in the river's waters rises from 60,000 to 1.5 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist2008-ganges-pollution_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-economist2008-ganges-pollution-145">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nomoreabuse_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-nomoreabuse-146">[138]</a></sup> with observed peak values of 100 million per 100 ml.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbraham2011_142-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbraham2011-142">[135]</a></sup> Drinking and bathing in its waters therefore carries a high risk of infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbraham2011_142-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbraham2011-142">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 1985 and 2000, <a href="/wiki/Rupee" title="Rupee">Rs.</a> 10 billion, around US$226 million, or less than 4 cents per person per year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[141]</a></sup> were spent on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganga_Action_Plan" title="Ganga Action Plan">Ganga Action Plan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cleanperish_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-cleanperish-20">[18]</a></sup> an environmental initiative that was "the largest single attempt to clean up a polluted river anywhere in the world".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-singh_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-singh-22">[d]</a></sup> The Ganga Action Plan has been described variously as a "failure",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mandal_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-mandal-150">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[i]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[j]</a></sup> a "major failure".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haberman_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-haberman-18">[a]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gardner_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-gardner-19">[b]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bharati_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-bharati-144">[h]</a></sup>
</p><p>
According to one study,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mandal_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-mandal-150">[142]</a></sup> </p><blockquote><p>
The Ganga Action Plan, which was taken on priority and with much enthusiasm, was delayed for two years. The expenditure was almost doubled. But the result was not very appreciable. Much expenditure was done on political propaganda. The concerning governments and the related agencies were not very prompt to make it a success. The public of the areas was not taken into consideration. The release of urban and industrial wastes in the river was not controlled fully. The flowing of dirty water through drains and sewers were not adequately diverted. The continuing customs of burning dead bodies, throwing carcasses, washing of dirty clothes by washermen, and immersion of idols and cattle wallowing were not checked. Very little provision of public latrines was made and the open defecation of lakhs of people continued along the riverside. All these made the Action Plan a failure.</p></blockquote>
<p>The failure of the Ganga Action Plan has also been variously attributed to "environmental planning without proper understanding of the human-environment interactions",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-singh_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-singh-22">[d]</a></sup> Indian "traditions and beliefs",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[k]</a></sup> "corruption and a lack of technical knowledge"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sheth_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-sheth-21">[c]</a></sup> and "lack of support from religious authorities".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-puttick_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-puttick-23">[e]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2009 the World Bank agreed to loan India US$1 billion over the next five years to help save the river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_Ganges_World_Bank_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Ganges_World_Bank-154">[143]</a></sup> According to 2010 Planning Commission estimates, an investment of almost Rs. 70 billion (Rs. 70 billion, approximately US$1.5 billion) is needed to clean up the river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cleanperish_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-cleanperish-20">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2008, the Ganges, alone among India's rivers, was declared a "National River", facilitating the formation of a <a href="/wiki/National_Ganga_River_Basin_Authority" title="National Ganga River Basin Authority">National Ganga River Basin Authority</a> that would have greater powers to plan, implement and monitor measures aimed at protecting the river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2014, the Government of India announced an integrated Ganges-development project titled <i>Namami Gange Programme</i> and allocated <span style="white-space: nowrap">₹</span> 2,037 <a href="/wiki/Crore" title="Crore">crore</a> for this purpose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[145]</a></sup> The main objectives of the Namami Gange project is to improve the water quality by the abatement of pollution and rejuvenation of river Ganga by creating infrastructures like sewage treatment plants, river surface cleaning, biodiversity conservation, afforestation, and public awareness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2017 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Uttarakhand" title="High Court of Uttarakhand">High Court of Uttarakhand</a> declared the Ganges River a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_personality" title="Legal personality">legal "person"</a>, in a move that according to one newspaper, "could help in efforts to clean the pollution-choked rivers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[147]</a></sup>  As of 6 April 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the ruling has been commented on in Indian newspapers to be hard to enforce,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mint_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-mint-159">[148]</a></sup> that experts do not anticipate immediate benefits,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mint_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-mint-159">[148]</a></sup> that the ruling is "hardly game changing",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hindu_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-hindu-160">[149]</a></sup> that experts believe "any follow-up action is unlikely",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-econtimes_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-econtimes-161">[150]</a></sup> and that the "judgment is deficient to the extent it acted without hearing others (in states outside Uttarakhand) who have stakes in the matter."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-outlook_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-outlook-162">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>The incidence of water-borne and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enteric" title="Enteric">enteric</a> diseases—such as <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_disease" title="Gastrointestinal disease">gastrointestinal disease</a>, cholera, <a href="/wiki/Dysentery" title="Dysentery">dysentery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_A" title="Hepatitis A">hepatitis A</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Typhoid" title="Typhoid">typhoid</a>—among people who use the river's waters for bathing, washing dishes and brushing teeth is high, at an estimated 66% per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbraham2011_142-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbraham2011-142">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent studies by <a href="/wiki/Indian_Council_of_Medical_Research" title="Indian Council of Medical Research">Indian Council of Medical Research</a> (ICMR) say that the river is so full of killer pollutants that those living along its banks in Uttar Pradesh, Bihar, and Bengal are more prone to cancer than anywhere else in the country. Conducted by the National Cancer Registry Programme under the ICMR, the study throws up shocking findings indicating that the river is thick with heavy metals and lethal chemicals that cause cancer. According to Deputy Director-General of NCRP A. Nandkumar, the incidence of cancer was highest in the country in areas drained by the Ganges and stated that the problem would be studied deeply and with the findings presented in a report to the health ministry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>Apart from that, many NGOs have come forward to rejuvenate the river Ganges. Vikrant Tongad, an Environmental specialist from SAFE Green filed a petition against Simbhaoli Sugar Mill (Hapur UP) to NGT. NGT slapped a fine of Rs. 5 crores to Sugar Mill also, a fine of 25 Lakhs to Gopaljee Dairy for discharging untreated effluents into the Simbhaoli drain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_shortages">Water shortages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Water shortages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Along with ever-increasing pollution, water shortages are getting noticeably worse. Some sections of the river are already completely dry. Around Varanasi, the river once had an average depth of 60 metres (200 ft), but in some places, it is now only 10 metres (33 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>To cope with its chronic water shortages, India employs electric groundwater pumps, diesel-powered tankers, and coal-fed power plants. If the country increasingly relies on these energy-intensive short-term fixes, the whole planet's climate will bear the consequences. India is under enormous pressure to develop its economic potential while also protecting its environment—something few, if any, countries have accomplished. What India does with its water will be a test of whether that combination is possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[155]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mining">Mining</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganges&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Mining">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illegal_mining_in_India#Illegal_mining_and_stone_crushing_in_the_Ganges_river_bed" title="Illegal mining in India">Illegal mining in the Ganges river bed</a> for stones and sand for construction work has long been a problem in <a href="/wiki/Haridwar_district" title="Haridwar district">Haridwar district</a>, Uttarakhand, where it touches the plains for the first time. This is despite the fact that quarrying has been banned in <a href="/wiki/Kumbh_Mela" title="Kumbh Mela">Kumbh Mela</a> area zone covering 140 km<sup>2</sup> area in Haridwar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tri_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-tri-167">[156]</a></sup>
</p>

<li><a href="/wiki/Ganga_(goddess)" title="Ganga (goddess)">Ganga (goddess)</a></li>
